Stagnant Japan Rolls Dice On New Era of Easy Money

Author: Dvorak, Phred; Warnock, Eleanor

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 21 Mar 2013: A.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/stagnant-japan-rolls-dice-on-new-era-easy-money/docview/1318073193/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: The stakes for Japan -- and the world -- are high. Since 1998, Japan's consumer prices have dropped to 1992 levels. The BOJ already pushed interest rates to near zero in 1999, then followed with several rounds of government bond-buying that have nearly doubled the central bank's balance sheet to around 166 trillion yen from 91 trillion yen in 1998.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Stagnant+Japan+Rolls+Dice+On+New+Era+of+Easy+Money&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Dvorak%2C+Phred%3BWarnock%2C+Eleanor&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   TOKYO -- Japan's new central bank governor, Haruhiko Kuroda, begins work Thursday on a feat no one before has managed: reversing nearly two decades of falling prices to lift wages and profits in the world's third largest economy.
Mr. Kuroda's predecessor, Masaaki Shirakawa, is among those who say it can't be done, at least not with the tools at hand. Using central-bank policies to boost stagnant prices is like "punching air," Mr. Shirakawa told parliament on Friday.
But backed by recently elected Prime Minister Shinzo Abe, the new central-bank governor has declared a mission to reach the bank's target of 2% inflation "as soon as humanly possible." Mr. Kuroda, 68 years old, is expected to begin buying trillions of yen more in bonds, along with other moves that echo strategies of the U.S. Federal Reserve and several other central banks.
The stakes for Japan -- and the world -- are high. Since 1998, Japan's consumer prices have dropped to 1992 levels. Wages are down 7%. Urban property prices are down 51%. Tax revenues are down 14%.
The Japanese economy has fallen to No. 3 from No. 2, behind the U.S. and China. An entire generation of Japanese have graduated from college, gotten jobs and, now approaching middle age, known only deflation.
Japan's weakness also has deprived the world economy of growth during the recessions and debt crises of the past decade. If the Bank of Japan experiment works, Japan could once again become an engine of global prosperity, as well as a more effective economic counterbalance to China.
Failure, however, could destabilize Japan's enormous government-bond market, and, by extension, its 2.2 quadrillion yen (about $23 trillion) financial system.
Even supporters of the new central-bank policies say Japan can't reverse course without significantly weakening the yen, an outcome that raises fears of a currency war. A weaker yen makes Japan's goods cheaper abroad, and the risk is that China and other nations might weaken their money in return.
Others aren't so worried. "Mr. Abe needs to do more and more. We're dying here!" said Masao Namiki, the 72-year-old president of a plastics mold maker in Tokyo. He hopes a weaker yen will revive firms, such as his, that have lost business to companies in China and other low-cost countries.
Mr. Namiki said he has just three shop workers, compared with 12 of them two decades ago. He shares machines with other mold makers rather than buy new equipment.
Japan's chronic deflation defies textbook solutions, and the debate has never been so heated. At one Tokyo shop, books on the topic battle for display space: "Reflation Is Dangerous," and "Deflation -- Explaining Japan's Chronic Sickness." Another is "America Knows That Japan's Economy Will Revive," by a Yale University economist and Abe adviser, Koichi Hamada, which asserts the BOJ has been out of step with modern global economic thought.
The shift to a more aggressive Bank of Japan could start as soon as Mr. Kuroda's first policy meeting in April, according to central-bank watchers. The BOJ could hold an emergency meeting even earlier.
Mr. Shirakawa, the departing central-bank governor, said the economy's problem is that Japan's population is shrinking, reducing demand for everything from cars to home mortgages. No matter how much cash the BOJ pumps into banks, the argument goes, money alone can't jump-start the economy because there aren't firms or consumers who want to borrow it.
That suggests the course followed by the Fed and other central banks to boost domestic economies -- low interest rates and so-called quantitative easing -- won't work in Japan. The BOJ already pushed interest rates to near zero in 1999, then followed with several rounds of government bond-buying that have nearly doubled the central bank's balance sheet to around 166 trillion yen from 91 trillion yen in 1998.
But Mr. Kuroda and Mr. Abe believe Japan suffers from deflation in part because consumers and businesses became accustomed to hoarding cash after the country's 1990s asset crash and lingering recession.
With few spending, demand falls, dragging down prices, profits and wages in a vicious cycle. They believe the solution is partly psychological -- persuading the Japanese to spend more today by convincing them prices are going up tomorrow.
"Mr. Kuroda has to sway the mind-set of the public," said Etsuro Honda, a former finance-ministry official and a chief adviser to Mr. Abe on BOJ strategy. "That's tough, changing people's psychology."
Mr. Kuroda has also said he favored buying longer-term bonds -- market watchers say 10- or even 30-year maturities -- which would in theory help drive down rates on longer-term bonds and business loans, encouraging investment.
So far, just talk of cranking up the BOJ cash machine, which started during Mr. Abe's campaign last fall, has made an impact. The yen has weakened 18% and stocks have risen more than 40% since mid-November on expectations of a change in central-bank policy.
The BOJ won't be tackling Japan's troubles alone. Mr. Abe has pledged 10.3 trillion yen in emergency government spending, as well as steps to boost long-term growth such as a bid announced Friday to join U.S.-led pan-Pacific free-trade negotiations.
Following a call by Mr. Abe for higher wages, such companies as Toyota Motor Corp. and convenience-store operator Lawson Inc. have announced bigger bonuses in the fiscal year that starts April 1.
One risk of the new policies -- dubbed Abenomics -- is that inflation, mostly through higher import costs, could push up prices for commodities and consumer goods well before an increase in profits and wages. That would turn into a drag on the economy rather than a push.
Energy prices, for example, went up 4% in the past year, and major utilities have said they are moving electricity prices up as much as 19% as nuclear power is increasingly displaced with imported fuel. Consumers say they are already paying more for everything from school lunches to swimming-pool fees.
In northern Iwate prefecture, Mikiya Yoshikawa, a manager at 48-car taxicab company Bunka Taxi, said most of the region's taxi firms are planning to raise fares about 9% in April, their first increase in 18 years, because rising fuel costs and fewer passengers mean they can no longer cover costs. Whether their price boosts spell better profits or lost business will depend on whether the broader economy picks up, said Mr. Yoshikawa.
"If the yen continues to weaken, inflation picks up, prices rise, then incomes rise -- that's all good. But there will certainly be a time lag" before the benefits get to consumers, Mr. Yoshikawa said. "If only prices rise but incomes don't, then people have no choice but to cut back on luxury items" like cab rides.
If inflation expectations push interest rates up before tax revenues have a chance to catch up, the government could find itself staggering under the interest payments on its quadrillion yen in debt.
Deflation has crippled the Japanese government's ability to corral its debt burden, already the worst among developed countries, at a huge 237% of gross domestic product and rising. Losing control over debt payments could undermine confidence in the Japanese bond market, scaring off investors and sparking a credit crisis bigger than Greece's.
"What I fear is that Japanese money might flee Japan," said Waseda University economist Yukio Noguchi, author of the hot-selling "Monetary Easing Will Bankrupt Japan."
A large portion of government bonds are held by Japan's banks, posing a threat if prices should plunge and yields soar. An October report from the International Monetary Fund estimated that a single percentage-point rise in interest rates would trigger paper losses on such holdings equal to 10% of key capital at major banks.
Japanese bond investors are skeptical. At Mizuho Trust & Banking Co., chief fixed-income fund manager Takehito Yoshino said he has been working in the industry since the early 1990s and can't remember a time when Japan had sustained inflation of 2% or more. He said he would continue to buy longer-term government bonds, since he doesn't expect consumer prices to rise much in the next few years. His only worry, he said, was central-bank purchases causing a bubble in the bond market that would eventually burst, crushing prices and spiking yields.
"They won't get a stable 2% rise in prices unless they create an asset bubble," similar to the one in Japan's highflying 1980s, said Mr. Yoshino.
In the far northern city of Sapporo, real-estate prices are finally rising, said Munekatsu Taguchi, an executive with Panda Real Estate, something he attributes partly to demand spurred by Japan's superlow interest rates. But he thinks higher demand will be limited to assets like stocks and real estate, doubting the bank will be successful in inducing an overall rise in prices. Home buyers, he added, are taking out floating-rate loans, a sign they expect rates to go lower.
Economists are divided over how best to navigate the various dangers. There is no precedent or proven formula to help Mr. Kuroda figure out how many trillions of yen the BOJ must produce to succeed. The more the central bank pumps out cash, the greater the impact on the yen, creating another path for rising prices and economic growth.
In one view, the weaker yen would make imports more expensive, pushing up prices. In Tokyo, Makoto Endo, secretary-general of the Wine Importers Association of Japan, said the weakening yen has already raised the cost of wine imports and left the association's 34 members resigned to raising prices when past inventory runs low in April or May.
A weaker yen also would boost profits for Japan's exporters by making goods cheaper abroad, eventually triggering higher wages and increased investment. Growing exports also would push up stock prices, encouraging people to put more of their money in the stock market instead of bank accounts.
Barclays economists estimate the Japanese currency has to depreciate roughly 30 yen against the dollar to reach the 2% inflation target in two years. Goldman Sachs estimates the dollar may have to rise to around 115 yen, from around 96 yen.
Japan has already been warned by German Chancellor Angela Merkel and Gao Xiqing, president of China's sovereign-wealth fund, against depreciating the yen too fast.
Early calls from Mr. Abe and his deputies for a cheaper yen prompted the Group of 20 major nations last month to issue a statement that members wouldn't try to lower their country's exchange rates below the others'.
Japanese officials now say only that the yen might weaken as a result of the central bank's monetary easing. The worry outside Japan is that the weakening yen helps trigger similar actions abroad, said Mohamed El-Erian, chief executive of investment giant Pacific Investment Management Co., or Pimco.
The debate is familiar ground for Mr. Kuroda, who spent most of his career on the front lines of Japan's battle to weaken the yen. He began as a disciple and then successor of Eisuke Sakakibara, the finance minister so well-known for talking down the currency that he was nicknamed Mr. Yen.
Mr. Kuroda's four-year tenure as Japan's currency czar was marked by 14 trillion yen in interventions to weaken the yen, as well as a running battle with the central bank, whose governor at the time privately favored a strong currency.
He continues to play to a tough audience. His former boss Mr. Sakakibara told investors Friday in Singapore that Japan's deflation couldn't be cured through monetary policy, and then joked that he was "worried about Mr. Kuroda."
---
Tatsuo Ito and George Nishiyama contributed to this article.

Bay Area: Wine Industry Tries to Fix Image in China --- California Vintners, Led by Bay Area Players, Promote Their Better Offerings in Market Where Cheap Stuff Sells at High Prices

Author: Worthen, Ben

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 21 Mar 2013: n/a.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/bay-area-wine-industry-tries-fix-image-china/docview/1318072059/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: "There's an amazing opportunity with this emerging middle class [in China] that's buying cars and watches and wine," says Linsey Gallagher, international marketing director for the Wine Institute.

Full text:   When Mark Bright attends wine events in China, he says, people often complain that California wine is overpriced and of poor quality.
The San Francisco-based winemaker and sommelier blames that reputation on vendors who have flooded China with cheap "plunk" wine that they sell at prices normally charged only for better vintages.
"It's horrible for California," says Mr. Bright, who laments that if this continues, "we're never going to be able to build a business in China."
California winemakers -- in particular those in Bay Area counties like Napa and Sonoma -- have spent decades establishing their wines as some of the best in the world. But in China, where drinking wine is a more recent phenomenon, these well-constructed wines compete with bulk wine from the state that is bottled, branded and sold at prices they could never command elsewhere.
California's wine establishment, led by Bay Area vintners, brokers and trade representatives, is now trying to change the image of the state's wine in China. Mr. Bright is working on a Mandarin-language book about California wines. The Wine Institute, a San Francisco-based advocacy group for California wines, in July 2011 started holding virtual tastings via video conference for Chinese journalists. And earlier this month, Gov. Jerry Brown announced plans to promote California wines on an upcoming trip to China.
There is a lot riding on the efforts. While China in 2012 accounted for just under $74 million of the $1.4 billion of U.S. wine exports -- about 90% of which comes from California -- that is up from $16 million in 2007 and $2.6 million in 2003, according to the Wine Institute.
"There's an amazing opportunity with this emerging middle class [in China] that's buying cars and watches and wine," says Linsey Gallagher, international marketing director for the Wine Institute. She adds that about 90% of the calls she gets these days are about China.
Some California wine businesses say they have seen firsthand how poor wines from the state have wound up in China. "We've had people just show up with bags of cash and say give me the cheapest wine. It was that bad," says Robert Dahl, chief executive of California Shiners, a Napa-based company that buys bulk wine and then blends it and bottles it for customers, who brand and sell it. The people, who are either from China or have business connections there, are often looking to make a quick buck, he says. He declined to name any of them.
Mr. Dahl started his company two years ago and says he is on track to ship one million cases of wine this year, almost all of it to China. He says that he takes steps to ensure that his is a high-quality product, including having the proper facilities to store and filter the wine.
"People were just sending junk," he says. "We've been pushing 100% against that. It makes us all look bad."
David Duckhorn, whose family once owned a Napa winery and who now lives in Shanghai, started a business importing wines to China in 2008 and has taken steps to distinguish the wine he sells from the lower-quality stuff. He says he only sells bottles that are branded the same as the ones a customer in the U.S. would get, from the same winemakers and sourced from the same vineyards.
Wine imported into China, like that brought to the U.S. from overseas, require a local-language label on the back. California winemakers would leave the back of their bottles blank or ship them with the Chinese-language labels already affixed. To demonstrate his wines are the real deal, Mr. Duckhorn now asks for winemakers he works with to ship bottles with an English-language label on the back and he sticks the Chinese import label over it.
"You can peel it off," he says, which lets buyers confirm the authenticity of the product.
Mr. Duckhorn's company, Via Pacifica U.S. Inc., will soon have eight offices in China, where his staff puts on tastings for Chinese buyers. He encourages them to check online the prices that the same bottles sell for in the U.S. so that they can tell whether it is overpriced.
A bottle of wine in China typically costs about two or three times what it would in the U.S. because of taxes, import fees and other costs.
Still, as a middleman Mr. Duckhorn can't always stop overpricing. One of his customers once bought some Decoy wine, a less-expensive release from the Duckhorn Winery -- formally owned by Mr. Duckhorn's family -- that retails in the U.S. for around $20 per bottle, and at a 10-times markup in China.
Ms. Gallagher of the Wine Institute says some of the current problems will go away as Chinese wine buyers become better educated. The institute has led trade missions to China with California winemakers to promote the wines and recently launched a Chinese-language website about California wines.
Ms. Gallagher says she has lately had some success marketing food-and-wine pairings, with California wines going well with spicy dishes and traditional Chinese food like barbecued meats. The Wine Institute has put on events with sommeliers and chefs to tout this in Beijing and Shanghai.
"Journalists [in China] now write about food-and-wine pairings and how good California wines are," she says.

Hog Prices Slide as Demand Wanes

Author: Berry, Ian

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 21 Mar 2013: C.4.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hog-prices-slide-as-demand-wanes/docview/1318071090/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: U.S. pork production this year has fallen 2% from year-ago levels, according to the U.S. Department of Agriculture, but that cut in supplies has been more than offset by lower exports, resulting in a rise in domestic pork inventories.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Hog+Prices+Slide+as+Demand+Wanes&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=C.4&amp;au=Berry%2C+Ian&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   CHICAGO -- Hog prices fell to a 4 1/2-month low, pressured by sluggish demand for pork among U.S. consumers and importers such as China and Japan.
Lean hogs for April, the front-month contract, slipped 0.35 cent, or 0.4%, to 77.875 cents a pound at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, as traders speculated Wednesday that slowing pork exports will cause U.S. supplies to swell. The price was the lowest settlement since Nov. 6.
Hog prices have slumped nearly 12% since early February, due in part to U.S. consumers switching from pork to cheaper meats such as chicken.
Analysts point to economic pressures on consumers, including a rise in gasoline prices and the expiration in January of the payroll-tax holiday.
Prices also have fallen because traders had expected U.S. pork exports to be stronger this year. The volume of pork exported from the U.S. fell 15% in January from a year earlier, as China, Japan, Mexico and other big buyers of U.S. pork cut back, according to the U.S. Meat Export Federation, a trade group.
The U.S. pork industry has become reliant on exports in recent years, reflecting in part China's growth in population and meat consumption. But while China, the world's biggest consumer of pork, was importing large amounts just 18 months ago, it has since built up its own hog supply and is relying less on U.S. supplies, analysts said. "They're not buying the pork, and we're ending up with plenty of pork here," said Art Liming, a futures specialist with Citigroup Inc. in Chicago.
Japan, typically the largest U.S. customer, has been importing less pork because of a struggling economy and weak currency, which has made it more expensive for its importers to buy U.S. pork. Russia, meanwhile, stopped importing U.S. pork in February, citing concerns about the medicated feed additive ractopamine, which is widely used in the U.S. to produce leaner meat.
Foreign importers bought 27% of the pork produced in 2012 in the U.S., up from just 7% in 2000, according to the Meat Export Federation.
U.S. pork production this year has fallen 2% from year-ago levels, according to the U.S. Department of Agriculture, but that cut in supplies has been more than offset by lower exports, resulting in a rise in domestic pork inventories.
Typically, pork demand starts to pick up this time of year as the spring grilling season and the Easter holiday approach. But even as food retailers were stocking up on hams ahead of the March 31 Easter holiday in recent weeks, wholesale prices for ham declined, according to U.S. Department of Agriculture figures, said John Kleist, senior analyst with brokerage Ebottrading.com in Lakemoor, Ill.
Cold weather across much of the Midwest and East Coast has added to the pressure on the market, as consumers have no reason to buy meat for the warmer grilling season, Mr. Kleist said.

U.S. Probes Microsoft Bribery Allegations

Author: Matthews, Christopher M; Palazzolo, Joe; Ovide, Shira

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 20 Mar 2013: B.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-probes-microsoft-bribery-allegations/docview/1317817214/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: The probe is one of dozens being conducted under the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act, a 1977 law that prohibits U.S. companies and companies that trade on U.S. stock exchanges from paying bribes to foreign officials. Since 2009, the Justice Department has brought 108 cases while the SEC has brought 77, yielding a total of more than $2 billion in penalties, according to the law firm Gibson, Dunn & Crutcher LLP.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=U.S.+Probes+Microsoft+Bribery+Allegations&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-20&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=B.1&amp;au=Matthews%2C+Christopher+M%3BPalazzolo%2C+Joe%3BOvide%2C+Shira&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Federal regulators are investigating Microsoft Corp.'s relationship with business partners that allegedly bribed foreign government officials in return for software contracts, according to people familiar with the matter.
Lawyers from the U.S. Justice Department and the Securities and Exchange Commission are examining kickback allegations made by a former Microsoft representative in China, as well as the company's relationship with certain resellers and consultants in Romania and Italy, these people said.
The investigation is in a preliminary phase, according to people familiar with the probe, and the government hasn't accused Microsoft or any of its business associates of wrongdoing. Such investigations can end with no charges being filed.
Spokesmen for the Justice Department and the SEC declined to comment.
"Like every large company with operations around the world we sometimes receive allegations about potential misconduct by employees or business partners," said John Frank, Microsoft's vice president and deputy general counsel. "We cooperate fully in any government inquiries," he added.
The probe is one of dozens being conducted under the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act, a 1977 law that prohibits U.S. companies and companies that trade on U.S. stock exchanges from paying bribes to foreign officials. Since 2009, the Justice Department has brought 108 cases while the SEC has brought 77, yielding a total of more than $2 billion in penalties, according to the law firm Gibson, Dunn & Crutcher LLP. The vast majority of FCPA cases settle.
The China allegations come from an anonymous tipster who passed them on to U.S. investigators in 2012, according to people familiar with the matter.
The allegations in China were also the subject of a 10-month internal investigation that Microsoft concluded in 2010, according to people briefed on the internal investigation.
The probe, conducted by an outside law firm, found no evidence of wrongdoing, these people said.
The tipster, who worked to land potential new business, alleged that an executive of Microsoft's China subsidiary instructed the tipster to offer kickbacks to Chinese officials in return for signing off on software contracts, according to people familiar with the matter and people briefed on the internal probe. The tipster, whose contract ended in 2008, was also involved in a labor dispute with Microsoft in China, they said.
Companies often spend millions of dollars investigating themselves for potential violations of anticorruption laws, then turn over the results to the government in the hope of getting lighter penalties or none at all.
U.S. government investigators are also reviewing whether Microsoft had a role in allegations that resellers offered bribes to secure software deals with Romania's Ministry of Communications, according to people familiar with the matter.
A ministry spokeswoman said the allegations were inconsistent with agency records and that ministry officials aren't privy to the business arrangements between Microsoft and its intermediaries.
The agencies are looking at Microsoft's dealings with consultants in Italy that specialize in customer-loyalty programs, the people familiar with the matter said. They have received allegations that Microsoft's Italian unit used such consultants as vehicles for lavishing gifts and trips on Italian procurement officials in exchange for government business, the person said.
Microsoft said more than 170 of its employees are responsible for investigating or monitoring compliance with company policies, including those related to FCPA.
"We take all allegations seriously and investigate them fully regardless of the source," said Mr. Frank of Microsoft. "We also invest heavily in proactive training, monitoring and audits to ensure our business operations around the world meet the highest legal and ethical standards."
Following the publication Tuesday of The Wall Street Journal's article about the bribery investigation, Mr. Frank wrote on a Microsoft blog: "The matters raised in the Wall Street Journal are important, and it is appropriate that both Microsoft and the government review them. It is also important to remember that it is not unusual for such reviews to find that an allegation was without merit."
A new whistleblower program at the SEC, established in the summer of 2010 with the passage of Dodd-Frank, has added fuel to the government's FCPA enforcement efforts.
The program provides cash incentives for employees to blow the whistle on securities violations, including breaches of the FCPA. If the SEC gets involved and finds wrongdoing after a whistleblower complaint, a tipster stands to gain as much as 30% of any monetary sanction the agency recovers. The agency's whistleblower office, which often works in conjunction with the Justice Department, reported receiving 115 tips alleging violations of the FCPA in the fiscal year that ended in September 2012.
Microsoft has offices in more than 100 countries, and the company disclosed it had about 94,000 full-time employees as of June 30. The company said in 2012 there were roughly 640,000 Microsoft partner businesses world-wide.
Overseas, as in the U.S., the company's largest chunk of sales are for software such as the Windows system that is the backbone of most computers in use at workplaces and government offices, and Microsoft Office, the bundle of software that includes Word for creating documents, Excel for spreadsheets and Outlook for email.

Credit: By Christopher M. Matthews, Joe Palazzolo and Shira Ovide

Location: United States--US Italy China Romania

World News: China's Xi Meets Treasury's Lew; A First for Both

Author: Paletta, Damian

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 20 Mar 2013: A.10.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-news-chinas-xi-meets-treasurys-lew-first/docview/1317816500/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: A U.S. official said after the meeting that Mr. Lew raised issues about the exchange rate, cybersecurity, intellectual property and North Korea.

Full text:   BEIJING -- In a symbolic but significant meeting, new Chinese President Xi Jinping and U.S. Treasury Secretary Jacob Lew acknowledged they wouldn't be able to settle all diplomatic differences but said the nations should continue to seek progress where possible.
The meeting was the first for Mr. Xi in his new role as president, as well as Mr. Lew's first meeting with a foreign leader as Treasury secretary.
Mr. Lew prodded China on familiar issues, such as reducing trade barriers to U.S. investment. A U.S. official said after the meeting that Mr. Lew raised issues about the exchange rate, cybersecurity, intellectual property and North Korea. They also discussed the global economy and the situation in Cyprus, the official said.
Mr. Xi told Mr. Lew the countries would "unavoidably" continue to have differences but said they could be minimized if Washington approaches the relationship in a "strategic and long-term" way.
The meeting serves as a test for Mr. Lew, known for his budgetary skills, but less so for his foreign-policy expertise. He dined with Finance Minister Lou Jiwei. and will meet Wednesday with Premier Li Keqiang and central-bank Gov. Zhou Xiaochuan.

Canada Firm Bets on Mexico Border

Author: Hudson, Kris

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 20 Mar 2013: C.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/canada-firm-bets-on-mexico-border/docview/1317815959/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: According to the government in Juarez's home state of Chihuahua, homicides in Juarez declined to 730 last year from 3,084 in 2010. Other large owners of Mexican border warehouses include U.S. real-estate investment trust Prologis Inc., Prudential Real Estate Investors and a joint venture of Kimco Realty Corp. and a Mexican firm.\n

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Canada+Firm+Bets+on+Mexico+Border&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-20&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=C.1&amp;au=Hudson%2C+Kris&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   CIUDAD JUAREZ, Mexico -- In this violence-plagued border town where thousands of workers assemble U.S.-bound goods, Brookfield Asset Management Inc. is betting that it can piece together an investment that was undercut by the U.S. recession and drug wars.
In December, Brookfield completed its purchase of an 81% stake in Verde Realty, a large property owner on the Mexico border, in a deal that valued Verde at $886 million. Brookfield declined to disclose how much it paid for the stake. Verde was founded a decade ago by real-estate mogul William Sanders largely on the belief that manufacturing along the border -- fueled by the 1994 North American Free Trade Agreement -- would gather steam.
But, with property values depressed on the Mexican side of the border, Verde turned into a financial disaster for the long list of high-powered investors that Mr. Sanders led into the company, including Vornado Realty Trust and Fortress Investment Group. Between 2003 and 2007, Mr. Sanders financed the company by selling shares for $25 to $33 each. The price paid by Brookfield and its partner, Ross Perot Jr.'s Hillwood Development Co., to buy the company: $13.85 a share.
Real-estate experts say that the value of industrial property has declined by as much as 10% in some Mexican border cities. "There was a discount for Mexican assets," said David Arthur, managing partner of Toronto-based Brookfield, which has $181 billion in assets under management, including real estate, infrastructure and energy.
Brookfield's bet also is based on its belief that a recovery is under way. Mr. Arthur points to a decline in drug violence and a strengthening of U.S.-Mexico trade. Hubs such as Juarez have been particularly important for assembling goods such as flat-screen televisions and all-terrain vehicles. Verde owns 18 million square feet of industrial property, one-third of it on the Mexican side of the border.
But these days, investing in Mexico is a contrarian bet. There are "a limited number of people who have an interest in Mexico" in the current real-estate climate, Verde Chief Executive Ron Blankenship explained to investors on a conference call last year about the Brookfield deal. "I realize there is frustration about this point, but that is the position we're in."
Mexican border factories, known as maquiladoras, sprouted in the 1960s, but their growth accelerated after Nafta made most trade between the U.S. and Mexico duty-free. Currently, nearly 1.5 million workers are employed in one or more of 2,800 maquiladoras in Mexico, according to John Christman, a retired analyst in Mexico City who tracks the industry. In Juarez, industrial space has grown to 60 million square feet from 29 million in 1997, according to CBRE Group Inc.
Yet, amid that growth there have been fits and starts. Maquiladoras stagnated and, in some cases, contracted in the early 2000s because of the U.S. recession then and China's entry into the World Trade Organization. Low-skill manufacturing, namely apparel, has migrated to countries such as China, Honduras and the Dominican Republic.
More recently, the strength of maquiladoras has been sapped by the global economic downturn and the drug war. The industrial vacancy rate in Mexico's border towns stands at 8.6%, nearly twice Mexico's national average, according to brokerage Jones Lang LaSalle.
On a recent visit to Juarez, there was ample evidence of the damage done by the drug war and poverty to the maquiladora dream. Outside the factories, some federal police patrolled the streets in vehicles bristling with high-caliber guns. Residents live in rows of tightly packed, dilapidated homes, and windblown garbage clutters the streets and open fields. The factories themselves have high perimeter fences, gatehouses and numerous security cameras. Workers at Verde facilities that were toured rarely venture outside during their shifts and eat in no-frills cafeterias.
Juarez has a 15% vacancy rate partly because of the recession and its reputation as the bloodiest venue for drug-cartel violence. According to the government in Juarez's home state of Chihuahua, homicides in Juarez declined to 730 last year from 3,084 in 2010.
Maquiladoras in Juarez screen prospective employees for criminal records and keep little cash on their premises. Many plants got rid of their ATMs in 2008 after a rash of robberies in which thieves stole machines and forced employees at gunpoint to give their security codes, according to accounts in the Mexican press.
In an interview, Mr. Blankenship said the weak economy was more to blame for Juarez's high vacancy than violence. "I'm not minimizing the violence, but I can think of only one tenant that we lost due to that."
Other large owners of Mexican border warehouses include U.S. real-estate investment trust Prologis Inc., Prudential Real Estate Investors and a joint venture of Kimco Realty Corp. and a Mexican firm. In December, a Mexican REIT created by Australian banking giant Macquarie Group debuted on the Mexican Stock Exchange and quickly amassed a portfolio of 224 industrial properties, most of them in Mexican border cities, in two deals totaling $1.5 billion.
Brookfield is counting on tenants such as manufacturer Viasystems Group Inc., which makes, among other things, energy-converter equipment to be installed in wind turbines. Homero Galindo, general manager of the 200,000-square-foot plant, noted that the clean, professional atmosphere in Juarez's industrial buildings differs from the city's gritty streets. "The violence pits the cartels one against the other," he said. "But the people who go to work every morning are very good."
---
Ana Campoy contributed to this article.

Full text:   TURKEY
Man Charged in Killing
Of New York Woman
A man suspected of killing a New York woman in Istanbul was charged with murder and ordered to remain in custody until his trial, the state-run news agency reported.
Sarai Sierra, a 33-year-old mother of three, was found dead near Istanbul's ancient city walls on Feb. 2.
The suspect, identified as 46-year-old Ziya Tasali, was caught near the Turkey-Syria border after being on the run for more than a month, mostly in Syria, officials said.
-- Associated Press
---
CHINA
U.S. Treasury Secretary
Meets With Xi Jinping
New China President Xi Jinping and U.S. Treasury Secretary Jacob Lew on Tuesday acknowledged they wouldn't be able to settle all diplomatic differences but said each country should continue to seek progress where possible.
Mr. Xi, in welcoming but direct remarks, told Mr. Lew that the countries would "unavoidably" continue to have differences but said they could be minimized if Washington approached the relationship in a "strategic and long-term" way.
Mr. Lew offered a polite but frank response, congratulating Mr. Xi on his new position while prodding China on issues such as reducing trade barriers to U.S. investment.
-- Damian Paletta
---
GLOBAL HEALTH
Nations Need $1.6 Billion
For TB Fight, Groups Say
Global health authorities said they need an extra $1.6 billion a year at minimum to fight tuberculosis in poor countries -- a large chunk of it to prevent and treat drug-resistant strains of the ancient, airborne disease.
The projection, from the World Health Organization and the Global Fund to Fight AIDS, Tuberculosis and Malaria, underscores the deepening epidemic of drug-resistant TB, the high cost of identifying and treating those complex and difficult cases, and the lack of health funding currently going toward it.
To identify all new cases of TB and step up treatment of drug-resistant strains over the next several years, the 118 low- and middle-income countries that qualify for international aid from the Global Fund need a total of $4.8 billion a year, the two health organizations said. That would allow treatment for 17 million people with all forms of TB, and save six million lives in 2014 to 2016, they estimated. The 118 countries can provide $3.2 billion of that annually themselves.
-- Betsy McKay
---
CANADA
Police Confirm Canadian
Aided Attack in Algeria
Canadian police confirmed at least one of the suspected hostage takers behind January's attack on the Algeria's In Amenas natural-gas plant was Canadian.
Algerian authorities and witnesses to the deadly four-day siege had said that at least one of the militants was described by other hostage takers as Canadian. The alleged Canadian militant had been part of a diverse group that included members from Algeria, Egypt, Tunisia, Mauritania, Libya, Niger and Mali, Algerian officials said.
-- Alistair MacDonald

Shale-Gas Boom Alone Won't Propel U.S. Industry

Author: Hagerty, James R

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 19 Mar 2013: B.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/shale-gas-boom-alone-wont-propel-u-s-industry/docview/1317573987/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Despite the benefits of lower energy costs, however, the game hasn't changed dramatically for most American manufacturers. Because of plentiful domestic supplies, natural-gas prices are likely to average $4 to $5 per 1,000 cubic feet over the next two decades, far below those in Europe, where they reached $10 to $13 recently, or northeastern Asia, where they have been $15 to $20, says Robert Ineson, who heads North American natural-gas research at IHS Global. The deficit in trade of manufacturing goods widened to about $687 billion last year from $673 billion in 2011, according to Manufacturers Alliance for Productivity & Innovation, a research group in Arlington, Va. Since the start of 2010, U.S. manufacturing employment has edged up 4.5%, to nearly 12 million, but remains far below the more than 17 million employed in 2000.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Shale-Gas+Boom+Alone+Won%27t+Propel+U.S.+Industry&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-19&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=B.1&amp;au=Hagerty%2C+James+R&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Cheap energy flowing from the U.S. shale-gas boom is often touted as a "game changer" for manufacturing. Despite the benefits of lower energy costs, however, the game hasn't changed dramatically for most American manufacturers.
Because of plentiful domestic supplies, natural-gas prices are likely to average $4 to $5 per 1,000 cubic feet over the next two decades, far below those in Europe, where they reached $10 to $13 recently, or northeastern Asia, where they have been $15 to $20, says Robert Ineson, who heads North American natural-gas research at IHS Global.
This energy-cost advantage is "a huge tailwind" for the U.S. economy, says John Faraci, chief executive of International Paper Co., a Memphis-based paper-and-packing maker.
Still, the price of natural gas is only one factor manufacturers consider when deciding where to locate plants. Taxes, labor costs and skills, regulation and government incentives also figure into the mix. Perhaps the biggest factor is long-term demand for goods. The biggest growth in demand is still expected to be in China, India and other emerging markets. Global companies typically want to locate production near their biggest customers.
International Paper's strategy illustrates the thinking of many global companies. The company spends roughly $1 billion a year on energy for 32 mills around the world, or about 10% of its total manufacturing costs. As a heavy energy user, International Paper expects benefits from lower gas prices, largely because cheaper energy should stimulate the entire U.S. economy, increasing demand for packaging.
But International Paper's competitors for American sales mostly also produce in the U.S. and will get the same energy-cost advantage. International Paper is putting most of its capital investment in such overseas markets as Russia, China, India and Brazil; it doesn't plan any new plants in the U.S., where demand for paper has been shrinking and packaging sales are growing only modestly.
"Our investments are really driven by demand, not costs," says Mr. Faraci, the CEO.
The story is similar at JM Eagle, a Los Angeles maker of plastic pipes with annual sales exceeding $1 billion. JM Eagle is getting a modest sales boost because some of its products are used in producing shale gas. In addition, gas is a feedstock for the resins JM Eagle buys as raw materials, so the company might be expected to benefit from low gas prices.
But Walter Wang, owner and chief executive of JM Eagle, says raw-material costs haven't fallen and probably won't. Instead, he says, resin producers are benefiting from higher margins while keeping prices steady. "They will find a way to keep the price stable because they can always export" if the domestic market is glutted, Mr. Wang says.
Though JM Eagle has always focused mainly on the North American market, it recently built its first plant in China and eventually will need at least 10 plants there, Mr. Wang says. He is investing more money in China than in the U.S. because the Chinese market has better long-term growth prospects.
For some companies, low gas prices are indeed changing the game. That includes makers of petrochemicals (whose raw materials include natural gas) and producers of materials or equipment used in energy production and exploration.
U.S. Steel Corp. has benefited from rising sales of pipes used in shale-gas production and distribution. Over the past two years, the company has upgraded its capacity for the kind of finishing work needed to make pipes rugged enough to be drilled thousands of feet into the earth; those investments include a new $100 million facility at its Lorain, Ohio, plant.
The shale-gas boom should help hold down electricity costs in the U.S. and may lower transportation costs too, helping the entire economy. BNSF Railway Co. recently said it plans to test using natural gas rather than diesel fuel to power locomotives. FedEx Corp. said it is likely to use natural gas for many trucks within the next decade, which in turn should spur investment in new types of engines.
So far, however, trade figures don't show an overall improvement in U.S. competitiveness: The deficit in trade of manufacturing goods widened to about $687 billion last year from $673 billion in 2011, according to Manufacturers Alliance for Productivity & Innovation, a research group in Arlington, Va. Since the start of 2010, U.S. manufacturing employment has edged up 4.5%, to nearly 12 million, but remains far below the more than 17 million employed in 2000.
Economists say the outlook for U.S. manufacturing has improved for several reasons. As wages in China rise swiftly, the labor-cost advantages of manufacturing there are diminishing. Meanwhile, U.S. companies have become warier of losing control of intellectual property in overseas plants and of relying on long supply lines overseas that can be disrupted by earthquakes or other natural disasters.
Now the gas boom is adding another reason for manufacturers to consider making things in America, at least to supply the North American market, says Dave Sievers, a principal at Hackett Group, a Miami-based consulting firm that advises manufacturers. But "it's not going to turn everything upside down," Mr. Sievers says. "It's not like the U.S. is going to be the workshop for the world."

World News: Japan Move Lifts Stakes For U.S. Trade Push

Author: Mitchell, Josh; Sekiguchi, Toko

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 16 Mar 2013: A.9.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-news-japan-move-lifts-stakes-u-s-trade-push/docview/1317459143/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: General Motors Co., Ford Motor Co. and Chrysler Group LLC once had a stranglehold on the U.S. market but have lost market share over the years to foreign competitors, including Japan's Toyota Motor Corp., Honda Motor Co., and Nissan Motor Co. Meanwhile, the market in Japan is dominated by its domestic auto makers, which U.S. companies blame on Japanese trade barriers.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=World+News%3A+Japan+Move+Lifts+Stakes+For+U.S.+Trade+Push&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-16&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.9&amp;au=Mitchell%2C+Josh%3BSekiguchi%2C+Toko&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Japan's move Friday to join talks on a trans-Pacific free-trade pact raises the stakes for U.S.-led efforts to expand trade and is sure to kick off a long battle involving industries, including autos and agriculture, that have long benefited from tariffs and other barriers imposed by their home countries.
A successful trade pact would alter advantages those industries enjoy in the U.S. and Japan, the world's No. 1 and No. 3 economies, respectively.
The Obama administration's top trade official, acting Trade Representative Demetrios Marantis, welcomed the move -- a bold bid by Japan to lift its flagging economy -- and said the two countries have been involved in preliminary discussions for a year. But he indicated Japan would have to make concessions.
"While we continue to make progress in these consultations, important work remains to be done," Mr. Marantis said.
The stakes are particularly high for each nation's auto makers. General Motors Co., Ford Motor Co. and Chrysler Group LLC once had a stranglehold on the U.S. market but have lost market share over the years to foreign competitors, including Japan's Toyota Motor Corp., Honda Motor Co., and Nissan Motor Co. Meanwhile, the market in Japan is dominated by its domestic auto makers, which U.S. companies blame on Japanese trade barriers.
Fifty members of Congress, almost all Democrats, asked the White House to take a tough line on Japan in considering whether to approve its entry into the talks. In a Thursday letter, the lawmakers singled out the fact Japan has the smallest share of auto imports among developed economies. About 6% of Japan's car sales are imports, while more than 40% of cars sold in the U.S. are imports, the letter stated.
"We've been trying for decades to essentially say to the Japanese that this has to be two-way trade," said Rep. Sandy Levin (D., Mich.), who led the drafting of the letter. "Nothing's worked."
Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe said joining the Trans-Pacific Partnership talks, known as TPP, is a "last chance" to take part in a rule-making process that could shift global trade. "For Japan to remain inward-looking means we are giving up on the possibility of growth," Mr. Abe said in Tokyo.
The move is part of a multipronged effort by Mr. Abe to lift Japan from a decadeslong economic malaise that also includes government stimulus spending and monetary easing by Japan's central bank.
Japan must win approval from the 11 nations involved in the Pacific trade talks, which isn't guaranteed. The country's bid to join the talks comes a month after the U.S. and European Union agreed to pursue their own sweeping trade pact.
The move also heightens pressure on the world's No. 2 economy, China, which isn't involved in the trade talks, to eventually alter trading practices other countries say have given Chinese manufacturers an unfair advantage in the global marketplace. "What I think it does do is say to China that higher-standard rules are increasingly going to be the standard in that region," said Edward Gerwin, a trade analyst at Third Way, a Washington think tank.
Friday's announcement comes after weeks of tension within Mr. Abe's Liberal Democratic Party, a pro-business conservative party with strong ties to farm associations that have significant influence over the rural vote.

Chinese Vitamin-C Suppliers Found Liable for Price-Fixing

Author: Kendall, Brent; Bray, Chad

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 15 Mar 2013: B.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/chinese-vitamin-c-suppliers-found-liable-price/docview/1316775334/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Attempts to blame the price-fixing on directives from the Chinese government ran counter to China's declarations to U.S. government agencies and the World Trade Organization that it doesn't compel price-fixing for any exported product, the plaintiffs argued.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Chinese+Vitamin-C+Suppliers+Found+Liable+for+Price-Fixing&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-15&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=B.1&amp;au=Kendall%2C+Brent%3BBray%2C+Chad&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Chinese manufacturers of vitamin C offered an unusual legal defense for why they shouldn't be found liable for price fixing in a recent civil trial: Their government made them do it.
A federal jury in Brooklyn didn't buy it.
On Thursday, after less than a day of deliberations, it found Hebei Welcome Pharmaceutical Co. and affiliated company North China Pharmaceutical Group Corp. liable for fixing prices on vitamin C for several years.
It was the first time Chinese companies had been on trial in the U.S. for alleged antitrust violations, according to Boies, Schiller & Flexner LLP's William Isaacson, who represented U.S. vitamin C buyers that brought the price-fixing allegations.
The judge in the case ordered the companies to pay $162 million in damages, triple what the jury had awarded. Antitrust violations like price-fixing are subject to treble damages.
"This verdict shows that companies in China are going to have to play by the same rules as everybody else," Mr. Isaacson said.
The vitamin C makers generally didn't dispute that they acted together to set prices. Instead, during the trial which lasted over two weeks, they argued that their government forced them to coordinate pricing and output.
China's Ministry of Commerce filed legal papers that supported the companies' position and urged that the case be thrown out, saying it couldn't be resolved without interfering with Chinese industrial policies.
The ministry also expressed concern that the U.S. court didn't properly defer to China's interpretation of its own domestic laws. Other companies facing similar allegations had settled the case before the verdict.
Lawyers who represented the defendants and the Ministry of Commerce didn't respond to requests for comment on Thursday. A spokesman for the Chinese Embassy in Washington declined to comment.
The case was brought by plaintiffs including a Texas animal feed company and a New Jersey vitamin distributor.
They alleged the Chinese vitamin C makers voluntarily formed an illegal price fixing cartel, one that increased prices significantly and imposed tens of millions of dollars in damages on U.S. consumers since late 2001.
The Chinese companies sold raw vitamins that were then used in the U.S. as an ingredient in food and beverage products, consumer vitamins and animal products, the plaintiffs said.
Attempts to blame the price-fixing on directives from the Chinese government ran counter to China's declarations to U.S. government agencies and the World Trade Organization that it doesn't compel price-fixing for any exported product, the plaintiffs argued.
The trial included testimony from Qiao Haili, a now-retired official from Beijing who worked at the China Chamber of Commerce of Medicines & Health Products Importers & Exporters.
Mr. Qiao, with a court-appointed interpreter huddled next to him in the witness box, testified that the Ministry of Commerce assigned him to the chamber, where he had authority over the companies' vitamin C production and export pricing.
"With respect to vitamin C, I had controlling means," Mr. Qiao said. "The government supported me toward the vitamin C."
The vitamin C makers said they had to follow directives issued by Mr. Qiao, while the challengers said Mr. Qiao wasn't a government official and the Chamber of Commerce where he worked wasn't a governmental organization.
Judge Brian Cogan ruled previously that Chinese law didn't compel the companies' conduct. He also declined to dismiss the case on grounds of enhancing relations between the U.S. and China.
Jeffrey Jacobovitz, an antitrust litigator with Arnall Golden Gregory LLP, said the companies' legal defense was "highly unusual, particularly when it involves the Chinese government."
Going forward, multinational Chinese companies "are going to have to carefully balance their responsibilities as global businesses with their obligations to and relationships with the Chinese state, recognizing that serious consequences may flow from tilting the scale too far in either direction," said John Terzaken, a former Justice Department antitrust lawyer now with law firm Allen & Overy.
Thursday's jury verdict comes more than a decade after similar price-fixing allegations were made against other foreign producers of vitamins.
In one of the largest-ever white-collar crime investigations, Swiss, Japanese and German companies were found to have conspired to raise vitamin prices in the late 1990s.
They were prosecuted in both the U.S. and Europe, paying $1 billion in criminal fines and more than $1 billion in civil judgments.
European and Japanese companies later exited the market, under pressure from Chinese companies that enjoyed lower production costs than their foreign competitors.
According to court documents, the U.S. market for vitamin C exceeds $100 million annually.

World News: Obama Raises Cybersecurity In Call to Beijing

Author: Gorman, Siobhan

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 15 Mar 2013: A.8.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-news-obama-raises-cybersecurity-call/docview/1316773976/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Mr. Obama's comments to Mr. Xi follow demands this week by his national-security adviser, Thomas Donilon, that China cease its backing of hacking to steal intellectual property from U.S. companies and that it work with other countries to develop common rules of behavior in cyberspace.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=World+News%3A+Obama+Raises+Cybersecurity+In+Call+to+Beijing&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-15&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.8&amp;au=Gorman%2C+Siobhan&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   WASHINGTON -- President Barack Obama in a congratulatory phone call to new Chinese President Xi Jinping raised the issue of cybersecurity, a strong sign of increasing focus on the issue in relations between the two countries.
In addition to discussing U.S. concerns about the North Korean nuclear threat, the White House said, Mr. Obama "highlighted the importance of addressing cybersecurity threats," which he cast as a "shared challenge." Mr. Obama framed cybersecurity in an economic context as the two countries address issues of trade, investment, and intellectual-property protections.
The White House didn't characterize Mr. Xi's response. China's state-run Xinhua news agency said Messrs. Xi and Obama exchanged views on the situation of the Korean peninsula and cybersecurity among other issues, and that Mr. Xi "expressed China's principles and positions on those issues."
Mr. Obama's comments to Mr. Xi follow demands this week by his national-security adviser, Thomas Donilon, that China cease its backing of hacking to steal intellectual property from U.S. companies and that it work with other countries to develop common rules of behavior in cyberspace.
Chinese officials deny that Beijing engaged in such activities. On Saturday, China's Foreign Minister Yang Jiechi called for cooperation on cybersecurity and said that China is a victim of cyberattacks. "Cyberspace needs not war, but rules and cooperation," he said.
Mr. Xi was confirmed as state president Thursday at an annual meeting of China's National People's Congress.

U.S. Steps Up Alarm Over Cyberattacks

Author: Gorman, Siobhan; Hughes, Siobhan

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 13 Mar 2013: A.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-steps-up-alarm-over-cyberattacks/docview/1316042734/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: In testimony before the House Intelligence Committee in February, Kenneth W. DeFontes Jr., chief executive of Baltimore Gas & Electric Co., told lawmakers that the electric industry takes cybersecurity "very seriously."

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=U.S.+Steps+Up+Alarm+Over+Cyberattacks&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Gorman%2C+Siobhan%3BHughes%2C+Siobhan&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   WASHINGTON -- The nation's top spies warned Tuesday of the rising threat of cyberattacks to national and economic security, comparing the concern more directly than before to the dangers posed by global terrorism.
U.S. intelligence officials told a Senate hearing that the nation is vulnerable to cyberespionage, cybercrime and outright destruction of computer networks, both from sophisticated, government-sponsored assault as well as criminal hacker groups and cyberterrorists.
"It's hard to overemphasize its significance," Director of National Intelligence James Clapper said, addressing members of the Senate Intelligence Committee. "These capabilities put all sectors of our country at risk -- from government and private networks to critical infrastructures."
Federal Bureau of Investigation Director Robert Mueller cited cybersecurity as something that keeps him awake at night, saying at the hearing it "has grown to be right up there" with terrorism.
The intelligence officials, in describing an annual inventory of global problems, didn't reveal imminent new cyberthreats or previously undisclosed plots.
But they amplified their warnings by casting them in terms usually reserved for threats emanating from al Qaeda and Iran, and they included projections of where the danger is expected to lead in the next two years.
The warnings came as part of an aggressive Obama administration campaign to draw attention to cybersecurity and to stir action to counter infiltrations and attacks that officials have said could allow foes to commandeer a nuclear-power plant or disrupt the financial system.
Last month, President Barack Obama signed an executive order aimed at bolstering computer-network protections, and he noted the "rapidly growing threat from cyberattacks" in his State of the Union address.
"We cannot look back years from now and wonder why we did nothing in the face of real threats to our security and our economy," he said then.
The following week, the administration rolled out a strategy to combat the theft of trade secrets. And Monday, in a speech in New York, National Security Adviser Thomas Donilon singled out China as a top perpetrator, demanding it adopt international standards of behavior in cyberspace.
Chinese officials deny that Beijing engaged in such activities.
On Saturday, China's foreign minister, Yang Jiechi, called for cooperation on cybersecurity and said that China is a victim of cyberattacks. "Cyberspace needs not war, but rules and cooperation," Mr. Yang said at a news conference. He said cyberspace shouldn't become a "new battlefield."
Mr. Obama discussed the issue with lawmakers when he met behind closed doors Tuesday with a group of Senate Democrats, participants in the meeting said. The administration push continues Wednesday when Mr. Obama holds a meeting with U.S. executives in the White House Situation Room to discuss cybersecurity.
But for all the collective worrying, there was little agreement between the Obama administration and Congress Tuesday over how to address the problem.
At a second Senate hearing, before the Armed Services Committee, lawmakers tussled over the role of the federal government in guarding against threats.
Army Gen. Keith Alexander, head of the U.S. Cyber Command, a part of the military, acknowledged that the Obama administration is debating internally how to proceed when U.S. companies are under cyberattack.
"The issue that we're weighing is: When does a nuisance become a real problem and when are you prepared to step in for that?" he said at the hearing. "That's the work that I think the administration is going through right now and highlighting that."
Lawmakers, too, acknowledged they can't agree on legislative measures to bolster protections for computer networks.
Last year, Republicans defeated a White House-backed bill that would have established voluntary cybersecurity standards for companies running critical infrastructure such as the electrical grid, citing concerns about a government role in cybersecurity.
Mr. Obama's executive order last month established voluntary standards as an interim measure, but the order lacks key incentives for companies to participate, like liability protections, that would require legislation.
The cost of protections remains another stumbling block, particularly for power companies, Gen. Alexander said, as he provided a relative ranking of computer protections in private industry.
"The banks and the Internet-service companies are pretty good; the power companies, not so good," Gen. Alexander said.
In testimony before the House Intelligence Committee in February, Kenneth W. DeFontes Jr., chief executive of Baltimore Gas & Electric Co., told lawmakers that the electric industry takes cybersecurity "very seriously."
Intelligence officials cited cyberassaults last year on the websites of many U.S. banks and a more destructive attack on a Saudi oil company that destroyed 30,000 computers as examples of the kind of disruptions already taking place.
They didn't discuss who mounted those attacks, but U.S. defense and intelligence officials have said the Iranian government is behind them. Iran has denied any involvement in the attacks.
"What we're seeing with the banks today I am concerned is going to grow significantly throughout the year," Gen. Alexander said at the hearing.
Looking ahead, Mr. Clapper said that chances of an ultrasophisticated attack capable of wiping out major nationwide computer networks are "remote." Countries most capable of carrying out such an attack -- China and Russia -- are unlikely to launch such assaults in the absence of a conflict or crisis, according to the assessment.
But even relatively unsophisticated hackers were projected by the intelligence officials of eventually being capable of disrupting insecure computer networks running parts of vital functions -- like the power grid.

World News: Chinese Economy Shows Weakness --- Early 2013 Data Signal Prices Rising, Activity Slowing in Driver of Global Economy; Forecasts Mixed

Author: Davis, Bob; Silk, Richard

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 11 Mar 2013: A.8.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-news-chinese-economy-shows-weakness-early/docview/1315532297/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Property sales have soared 77.6% from last year's levels in value terms over the first two months of 2013, while credit has also expanded rapidly. Because China's leaders repeatedly have said one of their top priorities is to keep a property bubble from reinflating, a continuation of these trends would put pressure on the central bank to clamp down.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=World+News%3A+Chinese+Economy+Shows+Weakness+---+Early+2013+Data+Signal+Prices+Rising%2C+Activity+Slowing+in+Driver+of+Global+Economy%3B+Forecasts+Mixed&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-03-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.8&amp;au=Davis%2C+Bob%3BSilk%2C+Richard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   BEIJING -- China's economy showed signs of weakness in the first two months of the year, falling short of expectations it would resume the kind of strong growth with low inflation that has helped drive a global economic recovery.
Statistics released over the past few days paint a picture of a China where inflation is mounting and domestic economic activity is slowing. Lending and property construction remain strong, but if the central bank senses that a credit-backed property surge is in the offing it could feel pressed to pinch credit, which could impede growth.
The disappointing recent numbers leave analysts divided about China's growth prospects. "The weak activity data and rising inflation reinforce our view that China's potential growth has dropped to 7% to 7.5%, if not lower," wrote Nomura analyst Zhiwei Zhang, who is more bearish than most bank economists who follow China. For 2013, he forecasts China's GDP will grow 7.7%.
Louis Kuijs, China economist at the Royal Bank of Scotland, who is on the optimistic end, also said the statistics had given him cause to rethink how well China's economy will perform. Since the end of 2012, "the data has been a little on the weaker side than you would have expected." But he says he hasn't changed his forecast that China will grow 8.4% in 2013.
It is hard to judge any economy by two months of data, especially when making adjustments for the Lunar New Year holiday, which was celebrated in January in 2012 and February in 2013. But with the world economy still in a fragile state, the strength of China's economy, the world's second largest after the U.S., takes on even greater importance.
China's departing premier, Wen Jiabao, last week said China was retaining its 7.5% growth target for the year. That number isn't considered a forecast, but a signal to provinces that the central government is convinced that the years of double-digit growth are over. China routinely exceeds its GDP target; GDP grew 7.8% in 2012.
For the fourth quarter of 2012, China reported that economic output rose 7.9% compared with the year-earlier period, halting seven consecutive quarters of declining growth. The acceleration from the third quarter, when GDP increased 7.4% year-to-year, eased concerns that China was in the midst of a downward spiral.
But since then, the economic news has been less rosy. On Saturday, China's National Bureau of Statistics reported consumer inflation jumped to 3.2% year-to-year in February, up from 2% in January, for the highest increase since April last year, though prices were likely boosted by the Lunar New Year, which often sees a spike in food prices and other goods.
Property sales have soared 77.6% from last year's levels in value terms over the first two months of 2013, while credit has also expanded rapidly. Because China's leaders repeatedly have said one of their top priorities is to keep a property bubble from reinflating, a continuation of these trends would put pressure on the central bank to clamp down. Already, a sharp climb in property prices prompted the government to pledge earlier this month to strictly enforce a 20% profit tax on property sales as a means of holding down speculative activity.
On a number of fronts, economic activity slowed early in the year. Industrial production grew slightly more slowly, with a 9.9% year-to-year rise in January and February, after a 10.3% gain in December. The government releases combined figures for the first two months of the year to avoid distortions related to the timing of the Lunar New Year.
Retail-sales growth slowed to 12.3% year-to-year in the first two months of the year, compared with a 15.2% gain in December. Nomura said that was the slowest growth in the January-February period since 2004.
The government's anticorruption effort may be taking a bite out of the luxury end of the retail market, a number of analysts said. Chinese leaders, most prominently new Communist Party Chief Xi Jinping, have been calling for an end to officials' expenditures on luxury.
Imports fell 15.2% in February, the government reported Friday, compared with the year-earlier period, another sign suggesting a slowdown in domestic demand, though the steepness of the trade could reflect the timing of the New Year. For the first two months of the year, imports were up a modest 5%, compared with a 6% gain in December.
Exports were a positive surprise, rising 21.8% in February, compared with the year earlier. For the January-February period, the gain was 23.6% year-to-year, compared with a December year-to-year increase of 14%. While some analysts said that presaged a strengthening of the global demand, others were more skeptical, saying the gains were out of line with more modest export gains in Taiwan and South Korea.
"We cannot conclude if this reflects genuine economic strength," said Nomura's Mr. Zhang. Mr. Kuijs of RBS said miscounting of exports could reduce the percentage gains by four or five percentage points.
