U.S. Coal Steams Offshore

Author: Johnson, Keith

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 07 Feb 2013: B.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/u-s-coal-steams-offshore/docview/1284581288/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: For U.S. coal-mining firms Peabody Energy Inc., Arch Coal Inc., and Alpha Natural Resources Inc. -- who together own Dominion Terminal Associates -- increased exports offer a way to partially offset declining domestic demand. Bruce Nilles, the director of the Sierra Club's anticoal campaign, said that 2012 was a "last gasp" for the coal industry and that economics will increasingly be against sending U.S. coal to Europe.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=U.S.+Coal+Steams+Offshore&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-02-07&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=B.1&amp;au=Johnson%2C+Keith&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   NEWPORT NEWS, Va. -- On 67 acres here along the James River, almost 800,000 tons of coal tower in two-story-high stacks, ready to go overseas. The shipping terminal is so busy that this past Christmas Day, workers volunteered for lucrative overtime to load a bulk carrier.
Coal-terminal operator Dominion Terminal Associates, owned by three big mining firms and one of the biggest coal exporters in the U.S., stands at the center of a surprising boom.
For all the troubles of the U.S. coal industry at home, its business with the rest of the world is brisk. Last year, the U.S. set a record for coal exports, with the final tally estimated to top 120 million tons, double what it exported as recently as 2009.
The boom isn't about feeding the voracious appetites of China and India -- not yet. Instead, American coal mined in the eastern U.S. and shipped overseas goes overwhelmingly to Europe, especially the U.K., the Netherlands and Italy.
British coal consumption for electricity rose about 50% in the third quarter of 2012 compared with 2011, as power producers there found coal cheaper than natural gas.
"The gas plant has simply been pushed off the grid by coal plant [in Europe], and that's most marked in the U.K.," said Nigel Yaxley, the managing director of the Association of U.K. Coal Importers, a trade group.
The rise in American coal exports is yet another side effect of the shale-gas revolution in the U.S., itself a result of advances in drilling techniques.
The glut of cheap natural gas has knocked coal off its dominant perch as a source of fuel for U.S. power plants. Tougher environmental rules are also squeezing coal mining in the U.S., and President Obama's inaugural address hinted at more greenhouse-gas regulation to come.
U.S. coal is finding a ready market in countries where natural gas is three to five times more expensive.
For decades, the U.S. has exported high-quality metallurgical coal used in steel mills. But now the growth is coming from steam coal, the kind that's burned in power plants.
Two years ago, barely one-tenth of the coal exported from the port here, known as Hampton Roads, was steam coal. Through the first nine months of 2012, steam coal accounted for about one-third of the total.
Rick Cole, the president of Dominion Terminal Associates, pointed out evidence of the shift on a recent drizzly morning. He carefully nosed his red Toyota Prius around sludgy puddles toward a ziggurat-shaped, crumbly mountain. "That's good steam coal right there, with all those big lumps in it. I would bet money that coal was mined in East Kentucky," he said.
The coal was waiting to be loaded on ships like the 92,000-ton Crystal Tiger, a Panama-flagged bulk carrier that before sunrise would swallow almost 40,000 tons of coal and head out into the Atlantic. A hand-painted motto dominates the stairway at the terminal headquarters: "Coal for the World."
Environmentalists say exporting coal amounts to sending U.S. greenhouse-gas emissions overseas. Groups such as Greenpeace and the Sierra Club have opposed half a dozen coal-export terminals planned for the West Coast to ship coal to China.
For U.S. coal-mining firms Peabody Energy Inc., Arch Coal Inc., and Alpha Natural Resources Inc. -- who together own Dominion Terminal Associates -- increased exports offer a way to partially offset declining domestic demand.
St. Louis-based Arch set a record last year with 13.6 million tons exported, nearly double the seven million tons in 2011. It has interests in new export terminals on the West Coast and the Gulf of Mexico, and it hopes to quadruple coal exports by 2020. Arch said this week that exports this year will remain at "elevated levels." Peabody, based in St. Louis as well, is also expanding coal-export terminals on the Gulf Coast.
In all, the U.S. exported $13.8 billion worth of coal in the first 11 months of 2012, including $3.8 billion of steam coal. Lower average prices for each ton of coal left total revenue down slightly from 2011.
Still, some forecasters are cautious about whether U.S. exports can keep rising. The U.K. importing group's Mr. Yaxley said tougher environmental rules and coal-plant phase-outs in the U.K. may start to erode cheap coal's advantage in 2015.
Europe's sluggish economy is another reason U.S. government energy forecasters and the mining association expect coal exports this year to decline slightly from 2012's record.
Protests against coal shipments to Europe are fewer than those against Asian deliveries because critics see the Europe boom as a short-term blip.
Bruce Nilles, the director of the Sierra Club's anticoal campaign, said that 2012 was a "last gasp" for the coal industry and that economics will increasingly be against sending U.S. coal to Europe. "Appalachian coal is going away. So where will the coal come from?" he said.
For now, though, the gloomy images of a coal-mining industry in decline feel far away in Hampton Roads.
"This cheap natural gas may be the best thing that's ever happened to this nation, when all's said and done, and this is coming from a coal person," said Dominion Terminal's Mr. Cole.

____________________________________________________________

Tensions Flare as Japan Says China Threatened Its Forces

Author: Hayashi, Yuka; Page, Jeremy; Barnes, Julian E

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 06 Feb 2013: A.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/tensions-flare-as-japan-says-china-threatened/docview/1284285383/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: The U.S., Japan's largest military ally, is bound by six-decade-old security treaty to defend Japan from any attack. [...]the Obama administration has made clear it is intent on winding down the wars the U.S. is involved in, not joining new ones.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Tensions+Flare+as+Japan+Says+China+Threatened+Its+Forces&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-02-06&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Hayashi%2C+Yuka%3BPage%2C+Jeremy%3BBarnes%2C+Julian+E&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Japan accused China's navy of locking weapons-guiding radar onto Japanese naval forces twice in the past three weeks, an escalation of their territorial dispute that has heightened fears of a military conflict between the Asian giants -- one that could entangle the U.S.
While no shots were fired, the radar at issue often precedes an attack. The incidents Japan described followed nearly two months of increasing tussles between the two air forces, including the first-ever reported intrusion by China into airspace claimed by Japan and the scrambling of advanced fighter jets by both sides.
The radar incidents "were cases that could have led to an extremely dangerous situation with just one wrong move," Japanese Defense Minister Itsunori Onodera said at a news conference in Tokyo Tuesday.
Diplomats and analysts say Chinese and Japanese political leaders are all anxious to avoid even a limited military confrontation, stressing that it isn't in the interest of Asia's two largest economies, linked tightly by trade and investment, to engage in a military battle.
A spokeswoman for the U.S. Defense Department, Maj. Cathy Wilkinson, said Tuesday that "we have seen and are concerned by the reports of [the Chinese radar] incident" and that the U.S. has long been encouraging all parties to avoid any step that could raise the risk of a miscalculation.
The U.S., Japan's largest military ally, is bound by six-decade-old security treaty to defend Japan from any attack. Moreover, the Obama administration has made clear it is intent on winding down the wars the U.S. is involved in, not joining new ones. Yet the U.S. also is committed to strengthening its defense ties with Japan as part of a strategic "pivot" toward Asia, which Beijing sees as a thinly veiled plot to contain China's expanding economic and military power.
The continuing tensions put Washington in a bind. While the U.S. doesn't want a war in Asia, it also can't risk weakening Japan and emboldening China without undermining its entire strategy in the Pacific. How the East China Sea island dispute is managed will have an impact on whether China's territorial disputes in the South China Sea grow more pointed. That has left Washington in a position to continue pressing for more talks between China and Japan, subtly reminding Beijing that while it takes no position in territorial disputes, it recognizes Tokyo's administrative control.
China's foreign ministry and defense ministry didn't respond to requests for comment Tuesday evening. Japan's foreign minister said Chinese officials responded to Tokyo's protests by saying "that they would first like to confirm the facts."
The increasingly testy military shadowboxing in the East China Sea between the two ancient rivals is complicated by the rise of new leaders in both countries trying to establish their security credibility, and by the shifting geopolitical dynamics of a region where China's military and economic power has already unnerved some other countries.
The military tangles mark a turning point in the emergence of China as a major military power apparently determined to reshape the U.S.-designed security architecture that has dominated Asia since 1945. Both Japan and China are intensifying military buildups, justified in part by the need to be prepared for conflict with the other.
"The region increasingly resembles a 21st-century maritime redux of the Balkans a century ago -- a tinderbox on water," wrote former Australian Prime Minister Kevin Rudd in Foreign Policy magazine last month, evoking the run-up to World War I.
Most analysts say a full-blown war is highly unlikely. But one worrisome element is a lack of direct communication channels and a code of conduct between the two nations' militaries. When Japanese military officials want to get in touch with counterparts in the People's Liberation Army, military attaches at the Japanese embassy in Beijing send a fax to the Chinese defense ministry, which can take days to respond.
Japanese and Chinese officials agreed last June to create emergency mechanisms to avoid accidental clashes in the East China Sea. The plan was to set up a hotline between defense leaders, to agree on a common radio frequency for vessels and aircraft to communicate in English when approaching each other, and to meet annually to discuss issues of concern. But no further talks have taken place since then.
All sides are mindful of an incident in 2001 when a Chinese fighter jet crashed after clipping the wing of a U.S. spy plane it was trying to intercept off the southern Chinese island of Hainan. U.S. officials couldn't make contact with the Chinese military or foreign ministry for several hours after the incident, said people familiar with it.
The U.S. has been pressing Japanese officials to try to get the hot-line talks moving again, figuring such agreements are key if low-level clashes are to be headed off before they become larger. "What you don't want is midlevel officers making decisions that force leaders of countries into bad strategic options," said a senior U.S. defense official.
Maj. Wilkinson, the Pentagon spokeswoman, said the U.S. has long counseled against moves "that raise tensions and increase the risk of miscalculations that could undermine peace and stability in the region."
At the State Department, spokeswoman Victoria Nuland said, "Actions such as this escalate tensions and increase the risk of an incident or a miscalculation."
A diplomatic thaw appeared to be emerging late last month, when a senior lawmaker from Japan's ruling coalition visited Beijing and personally handed a letter from Prime Minister Shinzo Abe to Xi Jinping, the new Chinese leader, raising expectations the two leaders might be open to summit talks.
At a parliamentary session on Wednesday, Mr. Abe said, "It is extremely regrettable that China took such provocative action unilaterally amid signs of emerging dialogue between Japan and China. He urged Beijing to "prevent the recurrence of such incidents and avoid escalating tensions needlessly."
As Japan's complaints show, there is no easy escape from the dispute over the small collection of uninhabited rocksknown as Senkaku in Japan and Diaoyu in Chinawith both sides finding it difficult to back down or give the other a face-saving out.
"We face continued provocations against our inherent land, waters, skies and sovereignty," Mr. Abe told Japanese troops Saturday as he surveyed a military base on Okinawa, a little under 300 miles from the area of dispute. He pledged to "confront the clear and present danger."
Mr. Abe has long been a prominent voice in Japan for taking a tougher line against China, and he followed his landslide election in December with a pledge to build up Japan's military, particularly in the East China Sea. Polls in Japan show both a rising dislike of China and growing popularity for Mr. Abe. Many Japanese, having watched their economy dwindle over two decades and feeling pressure from the Chinese and Korean economies, long to see a stronger nation again and are angered by China's assertive stance in the territorial dispute.
In China, Mr. Xi is under pressure from a fiercely nationalistic Chinese public that, while increasingly critical of corruption and abuse of power, insists the party protect China's territorial interests. Beijing blames the recent tensions on Tokyo's decision to purchase some of the islands from their private owner in September, and it is demanding that Japan abandon its longstanding position of refusing to acknowledge the territorial dispute.
"The Japanese side should stop the illegal activity of repeatedly sending ships and aircraft into the waters and airspace of the Diaoyu islands. . .and work together with China to find an effective way to appropriately control and resolve this issue through dialogue and consultation," Chinese Foreign Ministry spokeswoman Hua Chunying told a news conference earlier Tuesday when asked about Chinese ships operating near the islands.
Mr. Xi, who is the military chief as well as Communist Party leader, also is trying to establish credibility among Chinese commanders, many of whom believe the time has come for China to use its newly acquired firepower to assert itself in the region and beyond. Mr. Xi has taken personal charge of the territorial disputes by heading a "leading group" on maritime issues, say people familiar with the matter.
Uniformed commentators continue to shape Chinese public opinion over territorial matters, with several talking openly in recent weeks about the possibility of a war with Japan and conceivably even the U.S.
There has also been a spate of state media reports highlighting military exercises. The People's Liberation Army Daily last week described a recent military exercise that appeared to simulate a U.S. military intervention in a conflict with China. In the exercise, Chinese air force pilots were locked in combat with an imaginary enemy when they were surprised to hear a voice speaking in English over their radio, according to the newspaper.
However, there have been calls for restraint on the Chinese side in recent days. A Chinese general believed to have close personal relations with Mr. Xi was quoted in state media Monday as urging China not to allow another war with Japan to disrupt China's decadeslong efforts to modernize its economy and reclaim its status as Asia's dominant power.
"The construction of our economy has now entered a key phase, and should not be interrupted by a chance occurrence," Gen. Liu Yuan, political commissar of the PLA's General Logistics Department, was quoted as saying in the Global Times newspaper. He added: "The United States and Japan fear that we will catch up with them, and are trying every possible way to contain China's development, but we absolutely cannot be fooled by them."
In the radar incidents Japan reported Tuesday, Defense Minister Onodera said Chinese frigates aimed fire-control radar at a Japanese naval destroyer on Jan. 30 and at a navy helicopter on Jan. 19. While neither incident involved firing of shots -- the step that can follow use of such radar -- this was "highly unusual behavior" that occurs "only in extreme situations," he said. "We intend to push China very hard to restrain from engaging in such dangerous acts."
Japan's defense ministry said the radar targeting the Japanese destroyer -- Japan's 4,400-ton JS Yudachi -- came from a smaller Chinese ship, a Jiangwei-II class missile frigate. The ministry said another Chinese frigate, from the Jiangkai-I class, targeted the Japanese helicopter.
Japan didn't disclose where in the East China Sea the incidents occurred or how close they were to the disputed islands.
The report of the radar incidents followed a steady stream of increasing pressure from China around the disputed islands. Chinese maritime patrol boats have routinely entered territorial waters around the islands, flashing signs such as "Leave Chinese waters" and "Follow the Chinese law." On Jan. 8, such boats stayed in the territorial waters for 13 hours.
As Chinese maritime patrol and military surveillance planes flew into Japan's "air defense identification zone," Japan scrambled fighter planes against them 91 times in October through December -- the most since Tokyo started disclosing such data in 2005.
Like many countries, Japan has a long-standing policy of intercepting unidentified or potentially hostile aircraft entering its air defense identification zone, or ADIZ, which extends 200 miles from its shores. But China disputes the legality of Japan's ADIZ and the airspace Japan claims around the Senkaku/Diaoyu islands. Maj. Gen. Zhang Zhaozhong, a Chinese military theorist, said in a recent Chinese press report that Beijing needs to "break" Japan's ADIZ with its own aircraft, including fighters.
"If their fighters come and our fighters don't go out, from a diplomatic and military perspective, there is no reciprocity: It's not good," he said. "But if both sides bring out fighter jets, as to whether that might lead to a clash between them, that is another question."
Japanese officials have hinted that Japanese fighters could fire tracer bullets in front of intruding aircraft, an action they haven't taken since 1987, in response to an intruding Soviet plane. China's military hasn't responded officially to that suggestion. But Maj. Gen. Peng Guangqian of the Chinese Academy of Military Sciences was quoted by the China News Service as saying that "firing tracer bullets is a type of provocation. It's firing the first shot."
The watershed moment for Japan came on Dec. 13 when Japan said a small Chinese propeller plane violated the airspace over the disputed islands. The surveillance plane, which belonged to China's State Oceanic Administration, flew so low it escaped Japan's land-based radar on a nearby island. Dec. 13 was the anniversary of the 1937 Nanjing massacre, the symbol of Japanese World War II era atrocities in China.
On Jan. 10, China scrambled its own military jets after Japanese fighter planes chased after a Chinese patrol flying near the disputed islands, Japan said.
Within weeks of the Dec. 13 airspace intrusion, which was the first in decades by China, Japan's Mr. Abe unveiled his country's first military-spending increase in 11 years. Among items in the budget was a new radar to replace dated equipment that had missed the Chinese plane.

Full text:   Yum Brands Confronts Yuck Factor in China
You won't find "45-day chicken" in any cookbook, but it has become a potential recipe for disaster at Yum Brands Inc.
The term for preternaturally fast-growing birds allegedly pumped full of dangerous hormones threatens the fast-food giant's critical business in that country. An expose in local media may have dented same-store sales there by 6% during the final quarter after they grew by 9% through the first nine months of 2012. Yum has said its products are safe but that it will strengthen its supervision of suppliers.
Yum's full-year earnings, out Monday, will shed more light on the financial damage from the episode. The company has stuck to its forecast of at least 13% earnings-per-share growth overall for the 11th consecutive year. According to analysts' consensus, it will squeak by on its preferred earnings measures and will easily do so on reported figures at $3.38 a share. In 2011, it earned $2.74 on that basis.
But the danger that Yum's Chinese customers keep saying "yuck" highlights the country's importance.
China has been an almost uninterrupted success story since Yum opened its first KFC there in 1987. The company now has more than 5,400 KFC, Pizza Hut and other outlets there -- more than double what it had five years ago. And although that makes up only 14% of its total restaurants globally, those in China and other emerging markets are mostly company-owned, while only 11% of those in the U.S. are. Their contribution to the bottom line -- as well as the hit from a food-safety scare -- is therefore proportionally far higher.
Along with that threat has come greater opportunity and a correspondingly higher valuation multiple. Compared with rival McDonald's Corp., which has been slower to expand in emerging markets, Yum trades at a valuation premium. But the burger chain's shares have outperformed Yum's by 23% over the past two months in the wake of the food scare. That is despite its own weak fourth-quarter results.
Although niche operators can expand quickly, it is hard for a large, established fast-food brand to achieve much growth these days in mature markets. Developing countries hold the key. As Yum's shareholders are learning, though, what seems like a sweet opportunity can sour quickly.

World News: China's Surpluses Start to Even Out

Author: Back, Aaron

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 02 Feb 2013: A.9.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-news-chinas-surpluses-start-even-out/docview/1283433487/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: "Under a further reformed exchange-rate system where foreign reserves are stable, if there is a current-account surplus, there must necessarily be a capital- and financial-account deficit," it said in a statement accompanying the data.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=World+News%3A+China%27s+Surpluses+Start+to+Even+Out&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-02-02&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.9&amp;au=Back%2C+Aaron&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   BEIJING -- China's economic relationship with the rest of the world is becoming more balanced, new data show, as China soaks up less money from the outside world and as its central bank is intervening less in markets to keep down the value of the yuan.
The ratio of China's current-account surplus -- the broadest measure of its trade balance with the outside world -- to gross domestic product fell to 2.6% in 2012 from 2.8% a year earlier and more than 10% in 2007, the country's foreign-exchange regulator said Friday.
At the same time, China swung to a capital- and financial-account deficit, meaning there were net outflows of investment capital.
In previous years, China consistently ran large surpluses on both accounts, representing an influx of foreign funds that put pressure on the yuan to appreciate. To fight this appreciation pressure, the central bank, called the People's Bank of China, bought up the majority of the foreign currency that entered the country, leading to a swelling of the country's foreign-exchange reserves.
International critics pointed to the large "twin surpluses" and the accumulation of reserves by the PBOC as evidence that China was keeping its currency artificially cheap to give its companies a competitive advantage.
The 2012 data indicate China is moving toward a more normal macroeconomic state, typical of many economies around the world, where a surplus in one of the two balance-of-payments accounts is usually offset by a deficit in the other.
"Some people are worried about the capital-account deficit, saying that it shows outflows. But to me it only means one thing: that there is less central-bank intervention," said Barclays economist Huang Yiping.
"By definition, if you don't intervene significantly, a current-account surplus means you will have a capital-account deficit," he added.
China's foreign-exchange regulator, the State Administration of Foreign Exchange, had a similar interpretation of the data.
"Under a further reformed exchange-rate system where foreign reserves are stable, if there is a current-account surplus, there must necessarily be a capital- and financial-account deficit," it said in a statement accompanying the data.
In absolute terms, China's current-account surplus actually rose slightly to $213.8 billion in 2012 from $201.7 billion a year earlier, but economists say it is the ratio of the surplus to total GDP that is most meaningful.
Meanwhile, China swung to a $117.3 billion capital- and financial-account deficit, from a $221.1 billion surplus in 2011.
China's buildup of foreign-exchange reserves slowed considerably over the period. The country acquired $98.7 billion of foreign currency in 2012, down from $384.8 billion in 2011.
The data are preliminary and subject to revision, SAFE said.
In its statement Friday, SAFE said it can't rule out the possibility that foreign capital will begin flowing back into the country this year. In addition, if growth recovers in major foreign markets like Europe and the U.S., China's trade surplus could widen again, economists say.

Swedish Builder Finds Shelter in U.S. --- Skanska CEO Hopes for Lift From Politically Driven Demand for Health-Care Facilities, Cleaner Energy

Full text:   STOCKHOLM -- Two years after narrowing his focus to better concentrate on the U.S. market, Skanska AB Chief Executive Johan Karlstrom is hoping to catch a bit of a tailwind from President Barack Obama's second term.
The Swedish construction company, which has steered clear of emerging markets it views risky, is rolling in new American business contracts.
Already in the new year, it has announced $430 million in new U.S. deals and is scouring the Midwest, Texas and the West Coast, where it wants to increase its presence, for potential acquisitions.
Skanska is the seventh-biggest construction firm in the U.S. market in terms of new contracts.
The picture could get even brighter for Mr. Karlstrom. Having spent four years running Skanska's U.S. operations, the CEO expects the Obama administration to foster policies that boost the demand for additional health-care infrastructure, such as hospitals, outpatient centers and research laboratories, and upgrades in energy facilities.
Mr. Karlstrom said in a recent interview that Mr. Obama's re-election sent the company a "strong signal" that health-care overhaul will be implemented, leading to demand for additional hospitals and medical infrastructure.
The administration's drive for cleaner energy and energy independence also will benefit Skanska, he said.
In addition to energy and health care, Skanska is busy putting together subways, stadiums and prefabricated skyscrapers. The expansion of New York City's No. 7 subway line to Manhattan's West Side and the erection of the steel structure for the transit station at the city's new World Trade Center Transportation Hub are among its highest-profile projects.
Other recent megaprojects that bear Skanska's signature include New Jersey's MetLife Stadium, completed in 2010.
Over the past five years, the U.S. has represented some 30% of the company's global revenue, but that share is likely to rise as its expansion push bears fruit.
At the end of the third quarter of 2012, 44% of the company's order backlog of 148.7 billion kronor ($23.31 billion) was from the U.S., indicating the growing importance of the market for Skanska.
For Mr. Karlstrom, the U.S. construction market represents a haven amid choppy economic seas in Europe. And it is a safe bet compared with many of the world's promising but risky emerging markets.
Skanska knows something about getting burned by bad moves.
Just before Christmas, write-downs in Latin America reached 860 million Swedish kronor, including hefty charges related to cost overruns.
That is a large dent in profit for Skanska, which estimates its 2012 construction operating income in the neighborhood of $500 million.
After struggling to make projects in India and China profitable, the company retreated from the Asian nations in 2004.
Three years later, it pulled the plug on its operations in Russia, saying it was impossible to run a profitable operation in the country while avoiding questionable labor practices or corruption.
Other Western construction companies have struggled to establish a footprint in China, where large domestic players tend to outshine newcomers.
But some rivals, including U.K.-based contractor Balfour Beatty PLC, are looking to expand to new emerging markets to capitalize on the need for infrastructure construction.
"Theoretically the growth potential for us could be in India and China, but growing in the U.S., where we have the knowledge and size, carries vastly less risk," Mr. Karlstrom said.
The company will now be more prudent when taking on projects in Latin America, he added.
In late 2010, two years after moving back to Sweden from the U.S. to become Skanska's CEO, Mr. Karlstrom launched a growth plan that focused heavily on expanding U.S. operations even as the American economy was emerging from the recession. The effort has greatly increased reliance on the U.S. market for new business.
"The opportunities there are so huge for us" and Skanska is engaged in a record amount of bidding on American deals, he said.
Skanska is also open to growing in the U.S. through acquisitions. A year ago, it bought Industrial Contractors, an Evansville, Ind., company that builds natural-gas-fired plants, environmental control systems and other power and energy sources, and it is searching for additional acquisition targets.
But it hasn't all been a bed of roses. Skanska recently missed out on some of the largest infrastructure projects to enter the market in recent years, as competitors were chosen to build two bridges across the Ohio River, and a replacement for the Tappan Zee Bridge across New York's Hudson River.

Chinese Stocks Listing Overseas Get Floored in Frankfurt

Author: Mavin, Duncan

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 31 Jan 2013: C.10.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/chinese-stocks-listing-overseas-get-floored/docview/1282789236/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: On average, the 12 firms have lost about a third of their value since going public, and all but one has underperformed the broader German stock-market index.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Chinese+Stocks+Listing+Overseas+Get+Floored+in+Frankfurt&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-01-31&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=C.10&amp;au=Mavin%2C+Duncan&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   [Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Where there is a will, Chinese companies will find a way to an initial public offering. A few persistent Chinese firms have recently come to market in Germany, where Deutsche Boerse has welcomed them.
A miniwave of China-based issuers in Frankfurt last year brought the total number of Chinese companies listed there to 22. In the past two years, Chinese issuers outweighed all other foreign nationalities listing in Frankfurt, according to Dealogic. And while only two Chinese companies went public in the U.S. last year, seven debuted in Frankfurt, raising more money in aggregate than those in New York, Dealogic data show.
The deals included a metals maker, textile companies, a handbag manufacturer and a chemicals producer.
To be sure, the transactions remain small. Such deals have raised less than $500 million in total since late 2010.
But why Frankfurt? Deutsche Boerse has actively courted Chinese companies, citing Frankfurt's lower listing fees, easier qualification requirements and a faster IPO process compared with other major exchanges. The light regulatory touch extends to the free float of shares, too.
The average free float of Chinese companies that went public in Germany last year was only about 21%, and at shirt maker Firstextile, it is less than 10%. That means the pre-IPO owners keep a strong grip on their business.
In contrast, other locations were mostly shut off. Hong Kong's IPO boom between 2009 and 2011 ground to a halt last year after most of the newly listed companies underperformed the market. Partly as a result, the city's securities regulator has tightened the listing process. The U.S. appetite for smaller Chinese companies has waned, too, because of a series of high-profile accounting issues.
Chinese companies also have found their home turf difficult, with weak equity performance and bureaucratic stasis before last year's political transition causing a roadblock for potential deals.
So far, the performance of Frankfurt's Chinese stocks has been weak. Nine out of the 12 Chinese firms that have listed in Frankfurt since 2011 trade below their IPO prices. On average, the 12 firms have lost about a third of their value since going public, and all but one has underperformed the broader German stock-market index.
That will likely deter others from listing in Germany for the next two years, says Ulrich Plumbohm, of Shanghai-based merger adviser Plumbohm Corporate Finance.
The lesson for investors is that when relaxed governance or listings standards become a significant draw for emerging-market companies, controversy usually follows.
In London, governance issues at some emerging-markets resource firms eventually led to tighter listing rules. Several Chinese firms that listed on Nasdaq without an IPO have been delisted after allegations of accounting fraud at some of them, such as China MediaExpress, hurt the entire sector.
The dismal performance of Frankfurt's Chinese stocks not only suggests floating them in Germany wasn't a great idea, it may signal they should never have been listed at all.

Overheard: Fading Rationale

Abstract: "Despite economic and political uncertainty in the United States, continued economic turmoil in much of Europe and slower growth in China, Caterpillar Inc. today announced record 2012 sales and revenues," is how the company led off.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

Full text:   [Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Short of citing fears of a 2012 Mayan apocalypse, Caterpillar could hardly have set expectations lower than it did when reporting annual results on Monday.
"Despite economic and political uncertainty in the United States, continued economic turmoil in much of Europe and slower growth in China, Caterpillar Inc. today announced record 2012 sales and revenues," is how the company led off.
The apparent take-away: Lesser companies would have been stuck in the mud, but Cat powered through. Shame is, it went on to say that sales in the fourth quarter showed their first year-to-year decline since 2010, and earnings per share that fell short of analysts' estimates.
Cat shares still rose 2% and trade near the highest levels since last spring. And why not? With much of America's political uncertainty now behind it, Europe on the mend and growth picking up in China, all of the things that Cat and other global companies have said were holding them back look to be fading. But so, too, is the rationale for their excuses.

Foreign Money Pours Into Laos --- Neighboring Countries Jostle for Work in Resources and Hydropower; Western Companies Also Step In

Author: Otto, Ben

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 29 Jan 2013: B.8.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/foreign-money-pours-into-laos-neighboring/docview/1282224142/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: In a $9 billion economy, the injection of capital from abroad is an important part of the government's plan to expand budding industries by the end of the decade to shake off the country's status as one of the world's 48 least-developed countries. Citing the country's entry into the World Trade Organization next month "and the beginning of the integration of the country into the global economy, we see opportunities for growth in this market," the U.S.-based company says.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Foreign+Money+Pours+Into+Laos+---+Neighboring+Countries+Jostle+for+Work+in+Resources+and+Hydropower%3B+Western+Companies+Also+Step+In&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-01-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=B.8&amp;au=Otto%2C+Ben&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   VIENTIANE, Laos -- On the face of it, Laos is a no man's land for foreign investors.
This landlocked, Southeast Asian nation of 6.5 million people offers a tiny market. Experts say corruption is widespread. And some foreigners say they have been burned when deals in the country unraveled.
Yet investment is pouring in.
Foreign direct investment rose more than 40% in each of the past two years, largely as neighboring countries jostled for work in the resource and hydropower sectors. Chinese companies increasingly are breaking ground on real-estate projects, and Western companies including Coca-Cola Co. and French lens maker Essilor International SA also are stepping in.
The World Bank predicted that Laos would post a record trade deficit of $1.3 billion for last year. The gap would largely stem from imports related to foreign investments, such as fuel, vehicles and construction materials.
The Laotian economy remains small compared with those of neighboring Thailand, Vietnam and Myanmar. And foreign direct investment first topped the billion-dollar mark only last year.
But Laos hopes to attract $8 billion in investment from 2011 to 2015, the vast majority of it from foreign investors, to keep its economy expanding around 8% a year. In a $9 billion economy, the injection of capital from abroad is an important part of the government's plan to expand budding industries by the end of the decade to shake off the country's status as one of the world's 48 least-developed countries.
"Laos might not be as interesting as a Vietnam or Thailand, but it's growing, and it provides steady revenue," says Chris Manley, the Laos manager for RMA Lao, the largest U.S. company operating in Laos. The company, which distributes Fords, Land Rovers, John Deere tractors and heavy trucks and equipment, is building showrooms and expanding others to meet a car boom in the capital of Vientiane and in smaller cities.
Part of the appeal for foreign companies lies in establishing a footprint in the region as Southeast Asian nations prepare to form the Asean Economic Community in 2015. The bloc will bring together nearly 10% of the world's population into a shared economy of more than $2 trillion.
Coca-Cola in late November announced plans to build the U.S. company's first bottling plant in Laos. Citing the country's entry into the World Trade Organization next month "and the beginning of the integration of the country into the global economy, we see opportunities for growth in this market," the U.S.-based company says.
Malayan Banking Bhd. in November opened its first Laotian branch, in Vientiane. And Essilor, which makes eyeglass lenses, will build a plant this year in the southwestern province of Savannakhet, the company's first in the region beyond Thailand.
China CAMC Engineering Co. is working on Vientiane's new convention center, which is expected to be completed by November, and is a year into a $600 million plan to reshape Vientiane's waterfront over the next six years or so. The state-owned company long has played a construction role in Laos, a country with only a handful of buildings higher than five stories.
But whether these investors have moved too quickly remains a question. The U.S. State Department has warned that commercial law remains untested here. And risk-assessment firm Maplecroft said in a report this month that "the weak rule of law and frequent government intervention . . . will continue to expose foreign businesses to arbitrary legal action."
Several investments here have gone bust in recent years.
Macau-based Sanum Investments Ltd. says some of the casino operator's assets have been seized by the Laotian government in disputes between Sanum and its local partner and over roughly $23 million in taxes and fees. Sanum, a unit of Netherlands-based Lao Holdings NV, says the claims against it are baseless and that the government appears intent on expropriating the company's businesses, including one of the country's major casinos. Sanum says it invested more than $85 million in its Laotian operations and is seeking international arbitration.
Francis Chagnaud, a Frenchman who has been in the agricultural-forestry business in Laos for two decades, was told to relocate Agroforex Co.'s factory in Vientiane midway through a long-term contract when the state denied the company's landlord an extension. Mr. Chagnaud, who says the government has done little to address the issue, was forced to move when he found one day that part of his factory's roof had been removed overnight.
The Justice Ministry didn't respond to requests for comment about Sanum and Agroforex.
Mr. Chagnaud says the experience hasn't soured him on Laos, and that his troubles are typical in a developing country. "In Laos, the commercial competition may be less, but the state of law is undeveloped. And you should prepare investors for that," he says.
The government says it is working to improve the business climate and cites a streamlined bureaucracy and the country's recent acceptance into the WTO.
"It used to be that you had to go to multiple ministries to get licenses, and that's changed. We've allowed foreigners into most sectors -- hotels and hospitality and tourism -- and most industry, including mining and hydro," says Santisouk Phounesavath, a deputy director in the Ministry of Trade and Commerce.
"Thailand and China have always been willing to take more risks" in Laos, he says. "But now the laws are in place, so I hope this will attract more Western companies. Australia was the first to come. And companies from the U.S. and Europe, they're slowly coming too."
---
Wilawan Watcharasakwet in Bangkok contributed to this article.

Global Finance: Goldman to Trim Stake In China's Biggest Bank

Author: Ho, Prudence; Law, Fiona; Tudor, Alison

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 29 Jan 2013: C.3.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/global-finance-goldman-trim-stake-chinas-biggest/docview/1282224132/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: From HSBC Holdings PLC to Bank of America Corp., some of the biggest names in global finance have been busy unwinding their investments in financial institutions, partly in response to tougher rules on capital, and partly because of a view that the deals didn't deliver what they promised: an entry into a lucrative and promising market.

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Global+Finance%3A+Goldman+to+Trim+Stake+In+China%27s+Biggest+Bank&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-01-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=C.3&amp;au=Ho%2C+Prudence%3BLaw%2C+Fiona%3BTudor%2C+Alison&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   HONG KONG -- Goldman Sachs Group Inc. is raising around $1 billion from the sale of shares in China's biggest lender, Industrial & Commercial Bank of China Ltd., two people with direct knowledge of the matter said, in what could be the biggest share sale in Asia, excluding Japan, so far this year.
The Wall Street company is selling the Hong Kong-listed shares in a block trade at 5.77 Hong Kong dollars (74 U.S. cents) each, the people said, without disclosing the number of shares. The price represents a 3% discount to ICBC's HK$5.95 closing price Monday.
A person familiar with the situation said the sale reflects an effort by Goldman to manage its risks by reducing the size of its ICBC investment.
Volatility in ICBC's shares led Goldman to report a full-year net loss in Asia in 2011, its first since 2008. The U.S. bank wrote down the value of its investment in ICBC by US$1 billion in the third quarter of 2011 as shares in the Chinese bank tumbled, but recorded a US$99 million mark-to-market gain as the stock rallied in the third quarter.
The stake has been lucrative for the Wall Street bank. Following the current block trade, Goldman will have raised more than US$7.5 billion by selling shares in ICBC. The U.S. company still holds a small stake, the people said. Goldman first bought a 4.9% stake in ICBC for US$2.58 billion in April 2006, before the Chinese bank's Hong Kong listing.
The sale also will help boost Goldman's capital ratio ahead of tougher capital requirements. The Basel III capital rules, currently being implemented in stages world-wide, make it more expensive to hold minority stakes in financial institutions.
From HSBC Holdings PLC to Bank of America Corp., some of the biggest names in global finance have been busy unwinding their investments in financial institutions, partly in response to tougher rules on capital, and partly because of a view that the deals didn't deliver what they promised: an entry into a lucrative and promising market.
Last decade, at the time of their investments in China, many bankers hoped that the nation's financial markets would open up to foreigners quickly and that they could reach increasingly wealthy Chinese over their partners' networks. However, foreign banks still are severely restricted in what they can do in China and their ownership of Chinese financial institutions remains capped.
In some cases, analysts came to see the partnerships as one-sided, with the Chinese banks absorbing technical know-how from their U.S. and European counterparts with no clear benefits, beyond financial gains, in return.
Global companies sold about US$44 billion worth of shares in Asian financial institutions in 2012 to institutional investors or other strategic buyers, up from US$32.7 billion in 2011, according to Dealogic, a data provider.
The new move will be the fifth share sale in ICBC by Goldman since 2009. In April, Goldman sold US$2.3 billion worth of ICBC's Hong Kong-listed shares, or H shares, to Singapore state investment company Temasek Holdings Pte. Ltd., leaving it with less than 5% of the H shares, and 1.24% of the total share base.

World News: Seven Die in Bangladesh Fire

Author: Syed Zain Al-Mahmood

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 28 Jan 2013: A.16.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/world-news-seven-die-bangladesh-fire/docview/1282009724/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Concerned that not enough has been done to address workers' concerns, the U.S. Trade Representative's office has said it would decide by spring whether to withdraw, suspend or limit Bangladesh's duty-free benefits under a World Trade Organization arrangement that allows for special treatment of the world's poorest countries.

Full text:   DHAKA, Bangladesh -- A fire at a garment factory in the capital of Bangladesh killed seven workers, barely two months after a factory blaze left 112 workers dead and prompted widespread calls to improve safety in the sector.
Workers who survived the fire at the Smart Export garment factory in a Dhaka suburb on Saturday complained that the factory's emergency exit was locked when the fire broke out at around 3 p.m.. After the fire at the Tazreen Fashions factory in November, workers there also had complained of locked fire exits.
Rozina Begum, a 20-year-old sewing-machine operator, said the last thing she remembered before blacking out was being trampled.
"We all ran for the stairs, but the exit was locked," Ms. Begum said. "By the time the guards unlocked it, smoke had filled the room. There was a mad rush. I tripped and fell. People were stepping on my body. Then I lost consciousness. Someone must have pulled me out."
Sharif Ahmed, the owner of Smart didn't answer calls to his cellphone Sunday. Police officials said they hadn't been able to reach senior management of the factory.
Seven bodies were recovered from the burned-out factory, fire officials said. All of the dead were women. Six workers were being treated at the hospital, according to doctors, who said more than 50 received first aid, many after being trampled.
A senior fire official said the Smart Export factory, which occupied the second floor of a two-story building, appeared to have been operating without the requisite licenses and safety equipment.
The fire is another blow to the reputation of Bangladesh, the world's second-largest apparel exporter after China. Concerned that not enough has been done to address workers' concerns, the U.S. Trade Representative's office has said it would decide by spring whether to withdraw, suspend or limit Bangladesh's duty-free benefits under a World Trade Organization arrangement that allows for special treatment of the world's poorest countries.
One of the persistent problems that major retailers and workers-rights advocates complain about is the practice of suppliers' subcontracting to other factories, which can subvert the systems put in place by international retailers to audit factories for safety.
On a tour of the burned-out Smart Export factory Sunday, labels for the brands "Lefties" and "Bershka" were visible on garments on the factory floor. Both are labels owned by Inditex, a Spanish clothing giant.
Rezaul Karim, audit officer for Inditex's Dhaka office, said the company hadn't placed orders at the Smart Export factory. He said as a one-floor outfit renting a converted building with motor garages and a bakery on the ground floor, it was highly unlikely the factory could pass a safety audit by a retailer.
Jesus Echevarria, chief communications officer of Inditex in Spain said it appeared one of the company's Spanish garment providers, which was working with another contractor, subcontracted work to the factory.
He said the provider and contractor deny subcontracting. But since Inditex had indications Lefties and Bershka products were made there, the two companies have been suspended from Inditex's roster of suppliers. Inditex has sent a team from Bangladesh and another from Spain to investigate, he said.
Inditex is one of the world's biggest fashion chains, and Mr. Karim said the Dhaka office audits roughly 150 factories every year.
Following the Tazreen Fashions fire in November, the government promised to take action to improve working conditions in the country's $19 billion garment industry.
Mikail Shiper, a senior official of the Labor Ministry, acknowledged that the government was failing to monitor factories on the bottom rung of the supply chain.
"The Labor Ministry has only 16 inspectors to monitor upward of 5,000 factories," he said. "We're working as fast as possible," he said.
---
Christopher Bjork in Madrid contributed to this article.

____________________________________________________________

More than Yuan Reason to Like 'Dim Sum' --- Investors are Piling into the Bonds for Their Returns, Not Merely as a Wager that the Chinese Currency Will Appreciate

Author: McCarthy, Erin

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 25 Jan 2013: C.4.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/more-than-yuan-reason-like-dim-sum-investors-are/docview/1272440042/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Since November, the money manager has increased its allocation to dim-sum bonds in its Asian ex-Japan bond portfolios. Other investors say there are still plenty of reasons to be cautious around China's offshore bond market. Because it remains a small and nascent market, yuan-denominated bonds trade less frequently than dollar- and euro-denominated debt.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=More+than+Yuan+Reason+to+Like+%27Dim+Sum%27+---+Investors+are+Piling+into+the+Bonds+for+Their+Returns%2C+Not+Merely+as+a+Wager+that+the+Chinese+Currency+Will+Appreciate&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-01-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=C.4&amp;au=McCarthy%2C+Erin&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Borrowers tapping the market for "dim sum" bonds are starting to win over investors the old-fashioned way -- with higher yields.
For 16 weeks in a row, investors have piled into funds that focus on these bonds, which are denominated in yuan but traded outside mainland China, according to data provider EPFR Global. The $81 million of inflows in the week ended Jan. 16 were the second-highest since EPFR began tracking the data in 2011.
The growing investor appetite is a change from what was happening during much of 2012, when money was being yanked out of the dim-sum bond market.
Since dim-sum bonds began trading in 2009, most investors bought the bonds as a wager that China's currency would appreciate, lifting the value of the bonds. But that strategy backfired last year when China's government took steps to stem the yuan's rise, sending dim-sum bond prices tumbling. Yields move in the opposite direction of bond prices.
Now, companies are forced to sell bonds at higher yields to compensate for a yuan whose gains are less certain.
"Once the view on the currency changed, then the attitude on the bonds themselves started to change," says Edmund Harriss, a portfolio manager of the $90 million Renminbi Yuan & Bond Fund at Guinness Atkinson Asset Management. Borrowers "haven't got the currency to lean on in quite the same way, so they've got to issue something a bit more attractive" in terms of yield.
The fund has increased its exposure to dim-sum bonds to 65% from 41% at the middle of 2012.
The current average interest paid on newly issued corporate dim-sum bonds is around 4.25%, compared with 3.54% as of December 30, 2011, according to Barclays. That tops the 3.96% payments from U.S. corporate debt with similar maturities. Dim-sum bonds have returned about 2% over the last three months, Barclays said.
As well, companies that recently have tapped the $250 billion market generally have higher credit ratings than borrowers that had dominated in the market's earlier days, adding to the market's allure.
The shift in focus to bond yields, rather than just currency appreciation, is another sign that the dim-sum bond market is maturing just a few years after China began a push to liberalize trading of yuan-denominated instruments globally.
The bulk of issuers are companies based in mainland China and Hong Kong, and foreign companies such as Volkswagen AG that are looking to fund their operations in China.
Companies' dim-sum bonds tend to yield more than comparable debt denominated in major currencies like the euro and dollar. Caterpillar Inc.'s yuan-denominated bond due November 2014 currently yields around 2.37% compared with 0.51% on the company's November 2014 dollar bonds, according to data provider FactSet.
"You can get investment-grade quality . . . and the yield level is somewhere around 3%," says Angus Hui, fund manager of Asian fixed income at Schroder Investment Management in Hong Kong, where his team oversees $72 billion in assets. "That's quite attractive in today's environment."
Since November, the money manager has increased its allocation to dim-sum bonds in its Asian ex-Japan bond portfolios.
Other investors say there are still plenty of reasons to be cautious around China's offshore bond market. Because it remains a small and nascent market, yuan-denominated bonds trade less frequently than dollar- and euro-denominated debt. That means an investor looking to sell their bondholdings may have trouble finding a buyer at a desirable price.
"We haven't been rushing to accumulate credit denominated in [the offshore yuan]," partly due to the market's small size, says Adam McCabe, senior portfolio manager at Aberdeen Asset Management in Singapore. Aberdeen Asset Management oversees $314 billion in assets globally. Mr. McCabe says he prefers to buy yuan directly to gain exposure to China.
Investors in the past have tread carefully in the dim-sum market because many borrowers weren't rated by major credit-ratings firms. Little information about these borrowers was publicly available.
But that also is changing. In 2012, 57% of dim-sum bonds issued were sold by issuers with credit ratings, compared with 37% in 2011, according to Dealogic. Total issuance held steady last year compared with 2011 at $14 billion. That compares with $5.4 billion in 2010.
The yuan, though no longer the main attraction of the dim-sum bond market, continues to entice many investors eager to diversify away from the dollar and euro. China keeps it within a narrow trading band, reducing the risk of a sudden currency move wiping out bond returns. Strategists at Standard Chartered Bank now expect the yuan to rise about 2% against the dollar in 2013, after it edged up by 1% in 2012.
The yuan hit a record intraday high in mid January. Any further yuan appreciation likely will draw more investors to this market, since it would offer an added bonus to higher yields.
"The market has moved from [a foreign exchange] play to a credit play," says Michael Amorgianos, Standard Chartered Bank's director of the offshore renminbi syndicate desk, whose team arranges dim-sum bond deals.
Subscribe to WSJ:
Credit: By Erin McCarthy

Are Benefit Incentives Not to Work a Cause or a Result?

Author: Anonymous

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 25 Jan 2013: A.12.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/are-benefit-incentives-not-work-cause-result/docview/1272439592/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: The "great sucking sound" of manufacturing jobs going south that Ross Perot talked about during the 1992 presidential campaign has ended with Americans lined up to apply for food stamps and for any job regardless of what it pays.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Are+Benefit+Incentives+Not+to+Work+a+Cause+or+a+Result%3F&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-01-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.12&amp;au=Anonymous&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   Richard Vedder is incorrect in stating that one of the main reasons for lack of labor-force participation is government benefits ("The Wages of Unemployment," op-ed, Jan. 16). The opposite is true. The existence of growing government benefits, especially food stamps, is evidence of the lack of jobs that pay enough to live on without food stamps.
The lack of jobs, in turn, is due to the unrestricted importation into the U.S. of goods and services from low-wage countries at little or no tariff. The "great sucking sound" of manufacturing jobs going south that Ross Perot talked about during the 1992 presidential campaign has ended with Americans lined up to apply for food stamps and for any job regardless of what it pays.
When Hyundai Motor Group took applications for 877 assembly jobs in 2012, 22,000 people applied for those jobs that paid just $16 per hour. Similarly, Delta Air Lines took applications for 300 flight-attendant jobs in 2012 and received over 20,000 applications. Even McDonald's Corp., not noted for paying high wages, received over a million applications for 60,000 jobs. This is hard evidence that Americans are willing to work, not just live off government handouts as Mr. Vedder claims.
To restore decent wage-paying jobs to the U.S. we must return manufacturing to this country instead of importing everything from China, Mexico and other low-wage countries. I'm not talking just about assembly-line jobs. I'm talking about design, engineering, marketing and other linked jobs that support the entire manufacturing process.
Don Siefkes
Sterling Heights, Mich.
---
It must be nice to live in such a sheltered world that you can imagine, as Richard Vedder does, that Americans aren't working because they're so lazy they would rather collect food stamps or freeload through college on Pell Grants than get a job. Has Prof. Vedder ever tried feeding himself on $125 a month?
Most food-stamp recipients work or are caring for elderly or disabled family members. The real problem is that, aided by aggressive attacks on workers' right to organize, good-paying jobs have disappeared or been sent overseas, replaced by minimum-wage service-sector jobs that won't support a family.
What we need are policies that empower workers and encourage companies to provide adequate pay and benefits -- and to keep those jobs here, rather than offshoring both jobs and profits. The myth of the lazy poor may be comforting to some, but it doesn't reflect reality.
Orson Aguilar
Executive Director
The Greenlining Institute
Berkeley, Calif.
---
Mr. Vedder accounts for the full economic costs of subpar growth and high unemployment, pointing out the work-depressing effects at the margin of expanded federal benefit programs. Mr. Vedder's numbers suggest a vicious cycle whereby our weak economy creates fewer jobs, and fewer workers means more government benefits and more benefits lead to less-motivated job hunters, which means fewer employed taxpayers and a weak economy.
However, there is an equally important political side to the high-benefits, high-unemployment side of the ledger. President Obama won re-election with a popular-vote margin of a little more than three million. How many of the new food-stamp recipients (roughly 20 million since 2007), new recipients of Social Security disability payments (two million or so) and Pell Grants (about four million) and the long-term unemployed (about two million more receiving benefits for more than 26 weeks) voted for Republican candidates who wanted smaller government and spending cutbacks?
Democrats who look at the debit and credit sides of the government-dependency ledger get to feel self-righteous about their aid to the needy, and they get to retain political power as long they can avoid blame for lack of jobs and opportunities. On that score, they obscure the debits identified by Mr. Vedder by arguing that food stamps and unemployment benefits are extraordinary boosters of economic growth. That proposition may seem illogical (otherwise we all should receive benefits) and at odds with measly 1.8% growth, but enough voters apparently think they are better off with bigger government and more spending on benefit programs. Long-term economic and psychological costs of dependency are dwarfed by immediate political benefits to Democrats, and the wages of unemployment therefore aren't so troublesome.
Rick Collins
Washington.

Pyongyang Says It Plans

North Korea warned Thursday that it was prepared to conduct a nuclear test and carry out more long-range rocket launches.
In a statement carried by state media, the National Defense Commission in Pyongyang threatened to wage a "full-fledged confrontation" against the U.S. for what it calls continued hostility.

---
JAPAN
Trade Deficit Hit Record
$78.3 Billion Last Year
Japan's annual trade gap hit a record 6.927 trillion yen ($78.3 billion) last year, the finance ministry said Thursday, highlighting how a strong yen, a territorial dispute with China and surging energy imports took a toll on an economy driven by manufacturers.
The balance of trade in goods for December, released simultaneously by the ministry, came to 641.5 billion yen in deficit, mainly reflecting weak exports and rising imports. The reading for December was worse than a 548.5 billion yen gap predicted by economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires and the Nikkei, and up 208% from a year earlier.
While the yen has weakened by about 10% since November -- largely on expectations for aggressive credit easing advocated by new Prime Minister Shinzo Abe -- economists said it will take about half a year for the impact to take effect.
-- Takashi Nakami
---
CHINA
Manufacturing Expands,
In Latest Sign of Rebound
The preliminary HSBC China Manufacturing Purchasing Managers Index, a gauge of nationwide manufacturing activity, rose to 51.9 in January, compared with a final reading of 51.5 in December, HSBC Holdings PLC said Thursday.
A reading above 50 indicates expansion from the previous month, while a reading below 50 indicates contraction.
-- Richard Silk
---
SYRIA
Turkey Favors No-Fly Zone
But Admits Idea Is Unlikely
Turkey, long an advocate of imposing some kind of no-fly zone on Syria to stop aerial attacks by the regime of President Bashar al-Assad on Syrian cities, is still pushing the idea but seems to recognize that this option appears unlikely.
The idea of a no-fly zone has long foundered because of European and U.S. nervousness that enforcing it could broaden the conflict.
Turkish Foreign Minister Ahmet Davutoglu acknowledged that the military consequences of a no-fly zone could be "problematic."
-- Laurence Norman
---
THAILAND
Editor Sentenced to Prison
For Insulting Monarchy
A court in Thailand sentenced a magazine editor to 10 years in prison for publishing two articles that prosecutors said defamed the country's revered monarchy, focusing fresh international attention on both the way Thailand's strict lese majeste laws are applied and the extent of the country's gaping political divides.
Somyot Prueksakasemsuk was arrested in April 2011 near Thailand's border with Cambodia on charges that the now-defunct "Voice of Thaksin" magazine he edited had broken the law by running a pair of articles criticizing the influence and role of Thailand's constitutional monarch, King Bhumibol Adulyadej, who is regarded as almost divine by some Thais.
-- James Hookway
---
MEXICO
Court Orders Release
Of Frenchwoman Cassez
Mexico's Supreme Court on Wednesday ordered the release from prison of Frenchwoman Florence Cassez -- who had been sentenced to 60 years in prison eight years ago after being convicted of kidnapping -- because, the court said, her rights were violated in the case.
Justices voted 3-2 in favor of a proposal to throw out the case on grounds that Ms. Cassez's rights were violated during her arrest and the trial, particularly as a result of a staged televised version of her arrest. They ordered her immediate release.
-- Jose de Cordoba

World News: Shippers' Gloom Tells a Different Story on Exports From China

Full text:   SHANGHAI -- Many in the shipping industry see a sluggish year ahead for Chinese exports, a cornerstone of the world's No. 2 economy, despite other indications of growing demand for China's products.
Some executives from shipping companies that carry containers full of Chinese-made products mainly to Europe and North America say they see little evidence of a strong rebound in seaborne exports. Stephen Ng, director of trades for Orient Overseas Container Line Ltd., said the company wasn't seeing "any particular upsurge" in container volumes on its Asia-to-Europe or trans-Pacific services.
OOCL is the container-shipping unit of Hong Kong-listed Orient Overseas International Ltd. The parent said on Tuesday its container-shipping trans-Pacific services volume fell 6.8% in the fourth quarter compared with a year earlier. Volume on Asia-to-Europe services grew 2.8% compared with the year-earlier quarter.
Consulting firm Alphaliner expects modest demand growth this year, forecasting a 1% rise on Asia-Europe services and a 1.6% trans-Pacific rise. Last year, Asia-to-Europe volumes declined 5% while trans-Pacific volumes fell 0.4%, according to Alphaliner estimates. The vast majority of containers on both routes carry exports from China.
"We see a rally in shipping-company shares but the industry has not fundamentally improved," says Bonnie Chan, an analyst with Macquarie Securities, citing a weak trade outlook as well as overcapacity and low rates within the industry.
Chinese exports in December grew a surprising 14% year-on-year compared with modest growth of 2.9% in November. The measure was the latest in a spate of stronger Chinese economic indicators, culminating in Friday's report that China's growth in the fourth quarter accelerated for the first time in more than two years.
But many analysts believe the export numbers overstate the gain, and say the latest figures are influenced by exceptional factors such as backlogs caused by port strikes in the U.S. In a Thursday report, brokerage RBS said it saw data discrepancies related to trade with Hong Kong as well as with what are known as customs special-control areas, which are bonded areas that hold goods destined for export.
Both China's Commerce Ministry and customs officials defend the figures, with a ministry spokesman saying such fluctuations are common in trade data and noting a quarantine fee expired during the period. Zheng Yuesheng, a customs spokesman, said, "Every dollar in our statistical data corresponds to a real declaration bill."
Others said they see some growth. Tim Smith, Maersk Line's North Asia chief executive, said the company was experiencing "a rather unexpected strong pickup in exports" from China, especially to Europe, a rise he said looks set to continue to the Lunar New Year in mid-February.
Still, Mr. Smith said, "this doesn't seem to be a reflection of any underlying improvement in the state of the European economies."

World Economic Forum: Nations Try To Advance Modest Trade Deals

Full text:   With the dream of a big, global trade deal on life support, governments are focusing instead on less-ambitious trade measures that they hope will still give a needed boost to global growth.
In Davos, at the annual meeting of the global elite organized by the World Economic Forum that began Tuesday, trade ministers in recent years have taken stock of progress on the so-called Doha deal. Those talks at the World Trade Organization aim to produce a global accord that would reduce tariffs, eliminate regulations that restrict trade and cut farm subsidies.
But persistent disputes between developed nations, mainly involving the U.S., and developing nations, mainly China and India, have short-circuited the negotiations, leading most observers to conclude there is little chance that momentum for Doha will be revived soon.
"If the leaders of the European Union, the U.S., China and Japan want to revive the WTO, they can," said Gary Hufbauer, senior fellow at the Peterson Institute for International Economics in Washington. "If they don't, it won't happen."
While tariffs around the world have been falling steadily, regulations that limit trade in goods and services have become a rising source of friction between countries. These regulations are among the prime targets of trade negotiations that are moving forward separately.
One is a deal on free trade in environmentally friendly technologies, which would mirror an accord on information-technology products that governments signed in 1996. Another piece of the Doha deal that could move ahead on its own is harmonizing customs procedures and providing money for developing countries to bolster their customs infrastructure. Multinational corporations have thrown their weight behind the agreement.

Ahead of the Tape

Author: Jakab, Spencer

Publication info: Wall Street Journal , Eastern edition; New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]. 23 Jan 2013: C.1.

https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/ahead-tape/docview/1272050468/se-2?accountid=14426

Abstract: Yum, Burger King Worldwide Inc., Domino's Pizza Inc., Wendy's Co. and Sonic Corp. trade at between 18 and 35 times projected 2013 earnings and an average 60% premium to McDonald's, which fetches just 16 times.

Links: http://www.yidu.edu.cn/educhina/educhina.do?artifact=&amp;svalue=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;stype=2&amp;s=on

http://pqdt.calis.edu.cn/SearchResults.aspx?pm=0&amp;q=

http://159.226.100.141/Reader/union_result.jsp?title=1&amp;word=Wall+Street+Journal

https://tsinghua-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com.cn/openurl/86THU/86THU?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Aabiglobal&amp;atitle=Ahead+of+the+Tape&amp;title=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;issn=00999660&amp;date=2013-01-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=C.1&amp;au=Jakab%2C+Spencer&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Wall+Street+Journal&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text:   McDonald's 300-Billionth Burger Delayed
For people who appreciate precision, those "Billions and Billions Served" signs atop the golden arches are disturbingly vague. McDonald's Corp. stopped updating the number of burgers sold back in 1994 at 99 billion. But that didn't stop one blogger from estimating the total at 247 billion in March 2010.
Extrapolating from there, the chain was on track to crack 300 billion by this year. A recent slowdown has likely delayed that. Sales slowed throughout 2012 and, in October, McDonald's had its first year-on-year drop in comparable sales since 2003.
That affected Wall Street's assessment of full-year results, due Wednesday. Analysts see McDonald's reporting $1.33 of earnings per share for the fourth quarter, unchanged from a year earlier. As recently as April, the consensus forecast had been $1.46, according to FactSet.
A sales rebound in November as a result of new offerings such as cheddar, bacon and onion burgers and the reintroduction of the McRib in December likely salvaged the full quarter from a revenue decline. But menu tweaks will go only so far in addressing a fundamental problem: Fast food isn't the growth business it once was.
That goes not only for austerity-wracked Europe and the saturated U.S., but emerging markets, too. Even once red-hotYum Brands Inc., which shocked the market with a sales stumble and food-quality scare last year in China, looks vulnerable.
The upshot for McDonald's is that its stock is priced for this slower-growth world -- particularly having trailed peers this past year. Yum, Burger King Worldwide Inc., Domino's Pizza Inc., Wendy's Co. and Sonic Corp. trade at between 18 and 35 times projected 2013 earnings and an average 60% premium to McDonald's, which fetches just 16 times.
The "informal eating out" market is $1 trillion a year globally, according to McDonald's management, with fast food accounting for three-quarters of that. McDonald's already owns around 11% of its market but, with so many competing concepts around, struggles to grab a much bigger piece of that pie.
Growth will depend on new customers who don't yet have the disposable income to enjoy a Big Mac. There are billions and billions of them, but it may take a while before they stop by.
